Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Honor is preserved only by fixed punishment (hadd) regardless of the victim's chastity.
Correction: The text specifies that hadd applies only to slander of a chaste person; others receive discretionary punishment (ta'zir).

Key Takeaway
Every essential (religion, life, wealth, lineage, intellect, honor) is protected by a specific legal punishment prescribed in Islamic law.

The Five Necessities Preserved by Sharia
Commentator explains the preservation of wealth, lineage, intellect, and honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sharia preserves the five necessities (wealth, lineage, intellect, honor) through legislated punishments, and the meaning of each term as explained by the commentator.

Core Idea
Sharia preserves five foundations—wealth, lineage, intellect, honor—through punishments.

The commentator explains each term from the poem: wealth, lineage, intellect, and honor, along with the punishments legislated to protect them. This passage reveals how Islamic law prioritizes these essential human needs and considers their preservation obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand each term's definition and the punishment for its violation.
2
Identify the five necessities
Note that the passage covers wealth, lineage, intellect, and honor, and the final phrase indicates obligation. Notice the commentator's definition for each.
The five necessities are: wealth, lineage, intellect, honor, and religion? The passage mentions four plus the obligation; religion is not in this chunk? Actually the five are usually: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and wealth. Here only four are listed. The fifth (religion) may be elsewhere. But the passage says 'like them' for intellect and honor. So it's four plus the obligation. The title says 'five necessities' but the passage only covers four. However the poem may have mentioned religion elsewhere. We'll stick to what is in the chunk.

For each necessity, the commentator lists the punishment that protects it. Think about why that specific punishment is appropriate.
4

وقوله : (مَالُ) يُقرأ بسكون اللام وحذف الألف أي: ومال، فهو على حذف حرف العطف والمراد به كل ما يحلُّ تملكه شرعاً، وإن قل، ولحفظه شُرع حد السرقة، وحد قطع الطريق (۱). وقوله : (نَسَبْ) أي ونسب ، فهو على حذف حرف العطف، والمراد الارتباط الذي يكون بين الوالد ،وولده، ولحفظه شُرعَ حد الزنا . وقوله : ( ومِثْلُها عَقْلٌ) أي : ومثلُ المذكوراتِ عقل في وجوب الحفظ، ولحفظه حد شرب الخمر، والدية ممن أذهبه بجناية (٢) وقوله : وعِرْضُ ) أي : ومثلها عِرْضُ في وجوب الحفظ، وهو بكسر العين موضع المدح والذم من الإنسان وهو وصف اعتباري تقويه الأفعال الحميدة، وتُزري به الأفعال القبيحة، ولحفظه شُرع حد القذف للعفيف، والتعزير لغيره، فيُحد من قذف عفيفاً، ويُعزّرُ من قذف غير عفيف. وقوله : (قَدْ وَجَبْ) أي: حفظ الجميع، وقد عرفت الآكد منها، وإنما لم يرتبها الناظم على ترتيبها في الآكدية لضيق النظم.
And his saying: '(wealth)' is read with sukun on the lam and dropping the alif, i.e., 'and wealth' – it is by deletion of the conjunctive letter, and what is meant by it is everything whose ownership is legally permissible, even if small. And for its preservation the punishment for theft and the punishment for highway robbery were legislated (1). And his saying: '(lineage)' i.e., 'and lineage' – it is by deletion of the conjunctive letter, and what is meant is the connection that exists between the parent and his child. And for its preservation the punishment for adultery was legislated. And his saying: '(and like them is the intellect)' i.e., 'and like these mentioned is the intellect in the obligation of preservation.' And for its preservation the punishment for drinking alcohol and the blood-money from one who takes it by a crime (2) [were legislated]. And his saying: '(and honor)' i.e., 'and like them is honor in the obligation of preservation.' And it (honor) is, with kasra on the 'ayn, the locus of praise and blame in a person, and it is a notional attribute strengthened by praiseworthy actions and demeaned by blameworthy actions. And for its preservation the punishment for slander against a chaste person was legislated, and discretionary punishment for other than him. So one who slanders a chaste person is given the fixed punishment, and one who slanders one who is not chaste is given a discretionary punishment. And his saying: '(has become obligatory)' i.e., the preservation of all [these] is obligatory. And you have come to know the most emphatic among them. And the poet did not arrange them according to their order in emphasis only due to the constraints of the verse.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of a line from the poem dealing with the so-called 'five necessities' (الضرورات الخمس) that the Sharia aims to preserve. The commentator takes each word of the poem in order: First, 'مال' (wealth): it refers to anything lawful to own, and to preserve it the punishments for theft and highway robbery were legislated. Second, 'نسب' (lineage): the bond between parent and child, preserved by the punishment for adultery. Third, 'عقل' (intellect): preserved by the punishment for drinking alcohol and the blood-money required from one who destroys an intellect through injury. Fourth, 'عرض' (honor): a notional quality that is affected by actions, preserved by the punishment for slandering a chaste person and discretionary punishment for others. Finally, the phrase 'قد وجب' indicates that preserving all these is obligatory. The commentator notes that the poet did not list them in order of importance due to poetic constraints.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These necessities are merely worldly goods and have no religious significance.
Correction: They are divinely mandated priorities for legal preservation, as indicated by the obligation (قد وجب) and the specific punishments legislated.

The Ruling on Denying Necessarily Known Religious Matters
Commentator explains the death penalty for denying a necessary religious matter and its distinction from hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying a matter known by necessity from the religion is considered apostasy rather than a hadd offense, and the practical implications for burial and repentance.

Core Idea
Denial of a necessarily known religious matter is apostasy, not an ordinary crime.

This passage from the commentator explains a crucial distinction in Islamic law: the difference between a fixed punishment (hadd) for a sin and the penalty for apostasy. While both may involve death, the underlying reason and post-death treatment differ fundamentally. Let's examine the text to understand this important ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'known by necessity'
First, grasp the concept of 'matter known by necessity from the religion' — things every Muslim, scholar and layperson alike, knows must be true, like the obligation of prayer.
These are fundamentals of Islam that no one can claim ignorance of.
2
Identify the implication
Notice that the commentator states that denying such a matter entails rejecting the Prophet ﷺ, because these matters come from him definitively.
3
Distinguish from hadd
The commentator explicitly says this killing is not a hadd punishment. Hadd punishments are prescribed for specific sins and expiate sin, but apostasy does not — the person dies in unbelief.
This is a key legal distinction.
4
Learn the practical consequences
The footnote adds that one executed for apostasy does not receive the same treatment as a believer executed for a hadd: no washing, prayer, or Muslim burial.
The sin of a hadd offender may be expiated if accompanied by repentance, but not for apostasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمَنْ لِمَعْلُومٍ ضَرُورَةٌ جَحَدٌ مِنْ دِيننا يُقْتَلُ كُفْراً لَيْسَ حَدٌ) (من) مبتدأ، و (المعلوم) معمول مقدَّم لـ (جحد) ، واللام زائدةٌ لتقوية العامل فإنه ضعف بالتأخير، و (ضرورة) منصوب بنزع الخافض ؛ أي بالضرورة أو على التمييز ؛ أي: من جهة الضرورة، و(جحد) صلة (من)، و(من ديننا متعلق بـ (معلوم)، وجملة (يقتل) خبر، و (كفراً) منصوب على أنه مفعول لأجله، وليس) (حد معلوم مما قبله، لكنه أتى به توضيحاً، والمعنى : مَنْ جحد أمراً معلوماً من أدلة ديننا يشبه الضرورة، بحيث يعرفه خواص المسلمين وعوامهم، كوجوب الصلاة والصوم وحرمة الزنا والخمر ونحوها، يُقتل لأجل كفره؛ لأن جحده لذلك مستلزم لتكذيب النبي ، وليس قتله حدا وكفارة لذنبه كما في سائر الحدود ؛ فإنها كفارات للذنوب (۱) المقتول حدا يموت مؤمناً ، فيُغسل ، ويُصلَّى عليه، ويُدفن في مقابر المسلمين . أما المقتول لكفره والعياذ بالله فلا يُفعل ذلك به، إنما يُلقى كجيفة قذرة تطمر بالتراب . وقد يرفع الإثم عن المقتول حدا أو لا يرفع؛ لأنه إذا قتل وهو مصمم على العودة إلى مثل معصيته فهو مصر، والحد لا يطهره من رجسه، إنما الذي يطهره التوبة الصادقة، فإن اجتمع مع الحدّ تلك التوبة فقد رفع الإثم عنه، عنه، وإلا فلا انظر ردّ المحتار) (٥٩٧/١) و (١٤٠/٣).
His saying: 'And whoever denies from our religion a matter known by necessity is killed as unbelief, not a punishment' – (man) is a subject, (al-ma'loom) is an object advanced for (jahada), and the lam is extra to strengthen the agent because it is weakened by delay, and (darooratan) is in the accusative by removal of the preposition, i.e., 'by necessity' or as a specification, i.e., 'from the standpoint of necessity', and (jahada) is the relative clause of (man), and (min deenina) is attached to (ma'loom), and the sentence (yuqtalu) is the predicate, and (kufran) is in the accusative as an objective for it (object of purpose), and (laysa haddan) is understood from what preceded, but he brought it for clarification. And the meaning: Whoever denies a matter known from the evidence of our religion that resembles necessity, such that the elites of Muslims and their commoners know it, like the obligation of prayer and fasting and the prohibition of adultery and wine and the like, he is killed for the sake of his unbelief; because his denial of that entails denying the Prophet (peace be upon him). And his killing is not a hadd punishment and expiation for his sin as in other fixed punishments, for they are expiations for sins. (1) The one killed as a fixed (hadd) punishment dies as a believer, so he is washed, prayed over, and buried in Muslim cemeteries. As for the one killed for his unbelief (may Allah protect us), none of that is done to him; rather he is thrown like a dirty carcass covered with dust. And the sin may be removed from the one killed as a fixed punishment or not; because if he is killed while determined to return to the like of his disobedience, he is persistent, and the fixed punishment does not purify him from his filth; only sincere repentance purifies him. So if that repentance coincides with the fixed punishment, his sin is removed; otherwise not. Refer to Radd al-Muhtar (1/597) and (3/140).
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of a line from the poem. The commentator begins by parsing the Arabic syntax: 'man' (whoever) is the subject, 'li-ma'loomin darooratan' is an object advanced, 'jahad' is the verb, etc. The meaning is that whoever denies a matter that is necessarily known from the religion—i.e., known by both commoners and elites, such as the obligation of prayer, the prohibition of adultery—is to be killed for his unbelief, because such denial implies rejection of the Prophet. Importantly, this killing is not a hadd punishment (which expiates sin); rather it is for apostasy. The commentator then adds a long note clarifying the difference: one killed as a hadd dies as a believer and receives proper burial, while one killed for apostasy does not. He also discusses whether the hadd removes sin: it does not unless accompanied by sincere repentance.

Key Terms
معلوم من الدين بالضرورة
جحد
كفر
حد
تكذيب النبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Killing someone for denying a necessary religious matter is a fixed punishment (hadd) that expiates the sin.
Correction: No, it is not a hadd punishment; it is for apostasy and does not expiate sin. The person dies in a state of unbelief, and no Muslim burial rites are given.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the one who denies a necessarily known matter killed?
A) As a fixed punishment (hadd) to expiate his sin
B) Because his denial entails rejecting the Prophet
C) Because he is a highway robber
D) Because he does not perform his prayer

Key Takeaway
Denying a necessarily known matter of religion is apostasy, not a punishable crime with expiation; the individual is executed for unbelief and does not receive Muslim burial rites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly recognize the weight of denying something from Allah and His Prophet? Have you ever brushed aside a command or prohibition known clearly from the Quran and Sunnah, telling yourself it's not that serious? What would it mean for your faith if you consciously reject something that every Muslim knows is obligatory or forbidden?
?
This lesson says that one who denies a necessarily known matter dies in a state of kufr and receives no Muslim burial. How does that reality settle in your heart? Are there aspects of the religion that you 'know' but treat lightly in your actions, as if their necessity doesn't apply to you? Write down one such matter and make a plan to change your relationship with it.
?
The commentator stresses that the killing for apostasy is not a hadd that expiates sin. If you were to die in a state of major sin, would you depend on a legal punishment to cleanse you, or are you relying on Allah's mercy through repentance? What does this teach you about the urgency of turning back to Allah before death?

Takfir for Denying Consensus or Deeming Forbidden Permissible
Commentator discusses takfir for denying consensus or deeming forbidden permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which denying a consensus ruling or deeming a forbidden act permissible leads to unbelief, including the role of definitive consensus and matters known by necessity.

Core Idea
Not all denial of consensus leads to unbelief; it must be a matter known by necessity from religion.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies an important nuance in the rulings on unbelief (takfir). Building on the previous lesson about matters known necessarily from religion, he now addresses two specific cases: denying a consensus ruling and deeming a forbidden act permissible. As you read, pay attention to the conditions he sets—especially the distinction between definitive and silent consensus, and the requirement that the matter be necessarily known from the religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct cases the commentator addresses: denying a consensus and deeming permissible a forbidden act.
The passage includes a parenthetical footnote at the end; you may read it but focus on the main text first.
2
Identify the two main cases
The commentator first discusses someone who denies a consensus ruling (رجل نَفَى حُكماً مُجمَعاً عليه). Then he discusses someone who deems permissible a forbidden act (أو استباح مُحرَّماً). Under each case, note the conditions he gives.
3
Understand the consensus distinction
The commentator distinguishes between definitive consensus (إجماع قطعي) and silent consensus (إجماع سكوتي). Only denial of definitive consensus may lead to unbelief, and even then only if the matter is known by necessity from religion.
The poet's apparent view that any denial of consensus causes unbelief is considered weak.
4
Explore the 'necessarily known' condition
Both cases require that the matter be 'known by necessity from the religion' (معلوم من الدين بالضرورة). This is the key condition that limits takfir.
Examples: the obligation of prayer, prohibition of adultery, etc.
5
Examine the disagreement with some Maturidis
The commentator criticizes some Maturidis who distinguish between essential and incidental prohibitions, arguing that the incidental cause (like turning away from God's hospitality) is also necessary, so the distinction is flawed.
This part is more advanced; focus on the main point first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَمِثْلُ هَذَا مَنْ نَفَى لِمُجْمَعِ) أي: ومثلُ مَنْ جحد أمراً معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، مَنْ نفى حُكماً مُجمَعاً عليه إجماعاً قطعيًّا، وهو ما اتفق المعتبرون على كونه إجماعاً، بخلاف الإجماع السكوتي ؛ فإنه ظني لا قطعي، وظاهر كلام الناظم أن من نفى مُجمَعاً عليه يكفر، وإن لم يكن معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة؛ كاستحقاق بنت الابن السدس مع بنت الصلب، وهو ضعيف، وإن جزم به الناظم، والراجح أنه لا يكفر من نفى المجمع عليه إلا إذا كان معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة. وقوله : (أَوِ اسْتَباحَ كالزِّنَا ) أي : أو اعتقد إباحة مُحرَّم مُجمع عليه، معلوم من الدين بالضرورة، ولو صغيرة؛ سواء كان تحريمه لعينه كالزنا وشرب الخمر أو لعارض (٢) كصوم يوم العيد، فإن تحريمه لعارض، وهو الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى، خلافاً لبعض الماتريدية، حيث قال: من اعتقد حِلَّ مُحرَّم فإن كان تحريمه (٢) قوله : (أو لعارض إلخ) العلة هنا - وهي الإعراض عن ضيافة الله - لازمة، كما أن اختلاط الأنساب لازم للزنا، والإسكار لازم لشرب الخمر، فجعل تحريم صوم العيد عرضيا، وتحريم الزنا وشرب الخمر ذاتيا غير ظاهر، يُؤخذ ذلك من حاشية الشيخ الأمير. انتهى أجهوري .
His saying: 'And like this is whoever denies a consensus (mujma')' – i.e., like the one who denies a matter known necessarily from the religion is the one who denies a ruling that is agreed upon by definitive consensus (ijma' qat'i), which is that on which the considered scholars have agreed that it is consensus, unlike silent consensus (ijma' sukuti), for it is probabilistic, not definitive. And the apparent meaning of the poet's words is that whoever denies a matter of consensus becomes an unbeliever even if it is not known by necessity from the religion, such as the entitlement of the daughter of a son to one-sixth along with a direct daughter. But this opinion is weak, even though the poet asserted it. The sounder view is that whoever denies a consensus matter does not become an unbeliever unless it is known by necessity from the religion. And his saying: 'or deems permissible like adultery' – i.e., or he believes in the permissibility of a forbidden matter that is consensusually forbidden, known by necessity from the religion, even if a small sin; whether its prohibition is due to its essence, like adultery and wine, or due to an obstacle (m. 'arid), like fasting on the day of 'Eid, for its prohibition is due to an obstacle, namely turning away from God's hospitality. This is contrary to some Maturidis, who said: 'Whoever believes something forbidden to be permissible – if its prohibition is (2) His saying: 'or due to an obstacle etc.' – the cause here, namely turning away from God's hospitality, is a necessary consequence, just as mixing of lineage is necessary for adultery and intoxication is necessary for drinking wine. So making the prohibition of fasting on 'Eid incidental, and the prohibition of adultery and wine essential is not apparent. This is taken from the commentary of al-Amir. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains another line dealing with who becomes an unbeliever. It is a classification passage with a nuanced ruling. The commentator distinguishes two cases: first, one who denies a consensus ruling (ijma' qat'i). The poet's apparent view is that such denial leads to unbelief even if the matter is not known by necessity. But the commentator notes this is weak; the correct position is that it only leads to unbelief if the matter is necessarily known. Second, one who deems permissible a forbidden act that is consensusually forbidden and known by necessity, such as adultery or wine. The commentator mentions that this includes acts forbidden due to an obstacle (like fasting on 'Eid). He then criticizes some Maturidis who differentiate between essential and incidental prohibitions, arguing that the incidental cause here is also necessary, so the distinction is not sound. The note from the commentary of al-Amir is quoted in support.

Key Terms
إجماع قطعي
إجماع سكوتي
استباحة
معلوم من الدين بالضرورة
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying any consensus ruling makes a person an unbeliever, regardless of whether the matter is known by necessity.
Correction: The correct view is that denial of a consensus ruling only leads to unbelief if the matter is necessarily known from the religion (معلوم من الدين بالضرورة). Silent consensus denies probabilistic rulings.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, when does denying a consensus ruling lead to unbelief?
A) Whenever the ruling is agreed upon by scholars
B) Only if the ruling is known by necessity from the religion
C) Only if the ruling is about inheritance
D) Only if the ruling is based on the Quran

Key Takeaway
Unbelief for denying consensus or deeming forbidden permissible is restricted to matters known by necessity from the religion; not every consensus or prohibition triggers takfir.

Are there actions in your life that you conveniently consider 'permissible' despite knowing they are forbidden by clear evidence? What step will you take today to align your practice with what is necessarily known from the religion?
?

The Obligation to Appoint a Just Imam
Commentator explains the obligation to appoint a just imam, its Sharia basis, and the first condition of justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the appointment of a just imam is an obligation upon the Muslim community based on Sharia, not reason, and that the imam must meet the condition of 'adl al-shahada' which requires Islam.

Core Idea
The imam's appointment is a divine command, not a rational necessity.

This lesson examines the crucial position of Ahl al-Sunna that appointing a just imam is an obligation by Sharia, not by rational deduction. The commentator clarifies that this duty falls on the community when no explicit designation exists, and he begins to outline the required qualifications of the imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and grammar analysis
Start by reading the Arabic verse line and its grammatical breakdown to see how the commentator interprets the syntax.
Notice that 'obligatory' is a forward predicate, emphasizing the duty.
2
Understand the basis: Sharia vs. reason
The commentator explicitly states the obligation is by Sharia, not reason. Reflect on why this distinction matters in Islamic theology.
This counters the view of some rationalists who might argue for a rational basis.
3
Identify when the obligation applies and the different opinions
The passage lists three other scholarly opinions about the timing of the obligation. Find them and note the main view of Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Recognize the first condition of justice
The commentator defines 'justice' as 'adl al-shahada' and gives the first condition: Islam. Consider why a non-Muslim cannot fulfill this role.
The remaining four conditions are not in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمامة العظمى:
ووجوب نصب الإمام العدل ووجوب طاعته]
۱۳۰ - وَوَاجِبُ نَصْبُ إِمَامٍ عَدْلِ بِالشَّرْعِ فَاعْلَمْ لَا بِحُكْمِ العَقْلِ
قوله : (وَوَاجِبٌ نَصْبُ إِمَامٍ عَدْلِ) (واجب) خبرٌ مقدَّم، و(نصب) مبتدأ مؤخَّرُ؛ أي : ونصب إمامِ عَدْلٍ واجبٌ على الأمة عند عدم النص من الله أو رسوله على معين، وعدم الاستخلاف من الإمام السابق بخلافه عند النص من الله ، كما في قوله تعالى : "إِنَّا جَعَلْنَاكَ خَلِيفَةً فِي الْأَرْضِ" [ص: ٢٦] ، أو من رسوله أو الاستخلاف من الإمام السابق، كما وقع من أبي بكر، فإنه أوصى بالخلافة بعده لعمر رضي الله عنه . ولا فرق في وجوب نصب الإمام بين زمن الفتنة وغيره كما هو مذهب أهل السنة وأكثر المعتزلة.
وقيل : يجب لتسكين الفتنة ، وقيل : في غيرها؛ لأنه زمن الطاعة. وقيل: لا يجب أصلاً.
والمراد بـ (العدل) هنا عدل الشهادة ولا يتحقق إلا بشروط خمسة: الإسلام لأن الكافر لا يراعي مصلحة المسلمين.
The Supreme Imamate:
And the obligation to appoint a just imam and the obligation to obey him]
130 - And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory by Sharia, know, not by reason.
His saying: 'And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory' – 'obligatory' is a forward predicate, and 'appointment' is a delayed subject; meaning: The appointment of a just imam is an obligation upon the community when there is no text from God or His messenger designating a specific person, and when there is no delegation from the previous imam, contrary to the case when there is a text from God, as in His saying: 'Indeed, We have made you a caliph on earth' [Sad: 26], or from His messenger, or delegation from the previous imam, as occurred with Abu Bakr, for he bequeathed the caliphate after him to 'Umar, may God be pleased with him. And there is no difference in the obligation to appoint an imam between times of tribulation and other times, as is the school of Ahl al-Sunna and most Mu'tazila.
And it is said: It is obligatory to settle tribulation; and it is said: [obligatory] in other times because it is a time of obedience; and it is said: It is not obligatory at all.
And what is meant by 'justice' here is the justice required for testimony, and it is not realized except through five conditions: Islam, because the disbeliever does not look after the welfare of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on verse 130 of the poem, which deals with the imamate. The commentator begins by presenting the poem line: 'And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory by Sharia, not by reason.' He then analyzes the grammar: 'obligatory' is a forward predicate and 'appointment' is a delayed subject, meaning that the appointment is obligatory. He specifies that this obligation is upon the community when there is no explicit text (nass) from God or the Prophet designating a particular person, nor a delegation from the previous imam. He gives examples: God's text designating David as caliph, and Abu Bakr's delegation to 'Umar. He then states that the obligation applies equally in times of peace and tribulation, according to Ahl al-Sunna and most Mu'tazila. He mentions three other opinions: that it is obligatory only to settle tribulation, that it is obligatory only in non-tribulation times, and that it is not obligatory at all. Finally, he explains that 'justice' here means the justice required for legal testimony (adl al-shahada), which requires five conditions. He lists the first condition: Islam, because a disbeliever does not care for the Muslims' welfare. (The remaining four conditions are not given in this passage, likely continued on subsequent pages.)

Key Takeaway
Appointing a just imam is a Sharia obligation, not a rational one, and the imam must at least be Muslim to possess the required justice for testimony.

The source says a disbeliever cannot be just because he does not care for the welfare of Muslims. This is a serious criterion. Examine your own heart: Do you truly care for the welfare of your fellow Muslims, or do you sometimes prioritize your own interests over theirs? How does this lesson challenge you to be a better member of the community, even if you are not the imam?

The Obligation of Obedience to Rulers
The author explains the scope of obedience to rulers with a nuanced classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of obedience to rulers: when it is obligatory, when it is not, and the role of public benefit in permissible matters.

Core Idea
Obey the ruler in all but sin; permissible acts require public benefit to become obligatory.

This lesson unpacks the first line of the passage, which establishes the general rule of obedience to rulers based on the Qur'an and hadith. The author clarifies that obedience is not required in unlawful or disliked matters, and that permissible acts only become obligatory if they serve the public good, as illustrated by the example of banning tobacco.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic from the beginning up to 'فيحرم الآن' along with the literal translation. Notice how the author quotes the Qur'an and hadith to support the obligation.
The passage uses a technical style typical of classical commentaries.
2
Identify the three categories of actions
The passage divides actions into haram (unlawful), makruh (disliked), and mubah (permissible). Note the different rulings for each: the ruler is not obeyed in haram or makruh, but in mubah only if there is general benefit.
The example of tobacco shows how a permissible act can become obligatory due to public interest.
3
Reflect on the public benefit principle
Consider why the author emphasizes 'maslaha 'amma' (general benefit). This is a key principle in Islamic political theory: the ruler’s authority extends to matters of public welfare.
This principle is used to justify modern regulations like health bans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ (المُبِينِ أي : ولا تخرج عن امتثال أمره الواضح الجاري على قواعد الشريعة وفي كلامه حذف الواو مع ما عطفت، والتقدير (عن أمره ونهيه كما أشار إليه الشارح، ولو حمل الأمر في النظم على الشأن لعم الأمرين جميعاً، فتجب طاعته على جميع الرعايا ظاهراً وباطناً ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولى الأمر منكم) [النساء: 59]، وهم العلماء والأمراء، ولقوله : «مَنْ أطاعَ أميري فقد أطاعني، ومَنْ عصى أميري فقد عصاني» (١) ، لكن لا يُطاع في الحرام (١)، والمكروه، وأما المباح فإن كان فيه مصلحة عامة للمسلمين وجب طاعته فيه، وإلا فلا ؛ فلو نادى بعدم شرب الدخان المعروف الآن وجبت عليهم طاعته؛ لأنَّ في إبطاله مصلحة عامةً ؛ إذ في تعاطيه خِسَّةٌ لذوي الهيآت ووجوه الناس، خصوصاً إذا كان في القهاوي، وقد وقع أنه أمر بترك الدخان في الأسواق والقهاوي، فيحرم الآن(٢). قوله : (إِلَّا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ أي : إلا إذا أمر بكفر فاطرحن بيعته جهراً، فإن لم تقدر على الجهر بذلك فاطرحها سِرا . وقوله : (فَاللَّهُ يَكْفِينَا أَذَاهُ وَحْدَهُ أي : فالله تعالى يكفينا أذى الإمام الذي أمر بالكفر وحده؛ إذ هو الذي ناصيته بقدرته (۳) قوله : بِغَيْرِ هَذَا لَا يُبَاحُ صَرْفُهُ أي : بغير هذا الكفر من جميع المعاصي لا يجوز خلعه عن الإمامة لا جهراً ولا سرا . وقوله : (وَلَيْسَ يُعْزَلْ إِنْ أُزِيلَ وَصْفُهُ) بسكون اللام من (يعزل) للوزن؛ أي: وليس يُعزل إذا وُلّي مستكملاً للشروط، ثم أزيل وصفه السابق؛ وهو العدالة بطرو الفسق، خلافاً لطائفة ذهبوا إلى أنه يُعزل بذلك.
And his saying: 'And do not deviate from his clear command' meaning: do not leave complying with his clear command that proceeds upon the rules of the law. And in his speech there is omission of the waw with what it coordinates, and the estimation is 'from his command and his prohibition' as the commentator indicated. And if 'command' in the poem is taken to mean 'authority', it would encompass both matters. So obedience to him is obligatory upon all subjects, outwardly and inwardly, due to the saying of the Most High: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' [an-Nisa: 59], and they are the scholars and the rulers, and due to his saying: 'Whoever obeys my commander has obeyed me, and whoever disobeys my commander has disobeyed me' (1). But he is not obeyed in the unlawful (1) and the disliked. As for the permissible, if in it there is a general benefit for the Muslims, obedience to him in it is obligatory, otherwise not. So if he were to issue a call to abstain from the now well-known tobacco, obedience to him would be obligatory upon them, because in its abolition is a general benefit; for in its consumption is baseness for people of dignity and faces of people, especially if it is in coffeehouses. And it has occurred that he ordered the abandonment of tobacco in markets and coffeehouses, so it is now forbidden (2). His saying: 'Except with kufr, then throw away his covenant' meaning: except if he commands kufr, then discard his allegiance openly, and if you are unable to do so openly, then discard it secretly. And his saying: 'For Allah suffices us against his harm alone' meaning: Allah the Most High suffices us against the harm of the imam who commanded kufr alone; for He is the one whose forelock is in His power (3). His saying: 'Other than this, his removal is not permitted' meaning: other than this kufr, from all sins, it is not permissible to depose him from the imamate, neither openly nor secretly. And his saying: 'And he is not removed if his description is removed' with sukun on the lam of 'yuzal' for the meter; meaning: he is not removed if he was appointed complete in the conditions, then his previous description was removed—that is, justice by the occurrence of sin—contrary to a group who held that he is removed thereby.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on lines regarding obedience to the ruler. The author explains each line sequentially. First, he explains 'ولا تزغ عن أمره المبين' meaning do not deviate from the ruler's clear command, emphasizing that obedience is obligatory on all subjects based on Quran and hadith. He notes that obedience is not required in haram or makruh, and for mubah, it becomes obligatory if there is public benefit, citing an example of banning tobacco. Then, the exception 'إلا بكفر فانبذن عهده' clarifies that if the ruler commands kufr, one must reject his allegiance openly or secretly if unable. Next, 'فالله يكفينا أذاه وحده' reassures that Allah will protect from the harm of such a ruler. Then, 'بغير هذا لا يباح صرفه' emphasizes that for any sin other than kufr, deposing the ruler is not permitted. Finally, 'وليس يعزل إن أزيل وصفه' argues that even if the ruler becomes unjust (fasiq), he is not removed from office, contrary to some scholars. The passage thus establishes the Sunni position on obedience to rulers: obligatory except in clear kufr, and permanent tenure even after sin.

أمر بالمعروف
مصلحة عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a ruler must be obeyed in everything, including sinful commands.
Correction: The passage clarifies that obedience is not required in haram or makruh, and for mubah only if public benefit is present.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it obligatory to obey a ruler in a permissible action?
A) Always, because the ruler's word is law
B) Only if the action has general benefit for Muslims
C) Only if the ruler is just
D) Never, because permissible actions are optional

Key Takeaway
Obedience to rulers is obligatory in all lawful matters, but only for permissible acts that serve the public good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When have you justified disobeying a ruler based on a personal whim, ignoring the condition of public benefit? How would this passage change your future response to regulations you dislike?

When to Disobey: Kufr and the Permanence of Rule
The author explains the sole exception to obedience and the rule against removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the only justification for disobeying and deposing a ruler is when he commands kufr, and that a ruler is not removed for becoming unjust or sinful.

Core Idea
Only kufr breaks the bond of allegiance; a sinful ruler remains in power.

This lesson covers the remaining lines of the passage, which deal with the exception for kufr and the ruling on the ruler's removal. The author emphasizes that allegiance is only discarded if the ruler commands clear kufr, and in that case one must oppose him openly if possible, or secretly if not. Furthermore, he states that no other sin justifies removal, and even if the ruler becomes unjust (fasiq), he is not automatically dismissed, contrary to some scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic lines on kufr and the final ruling
Read from 'إلا بكفر' to the end of the passage along with the translation. Note the strong language: 'فانبذن عهده' (discard his covenant) and 'فالله يكفينا أذاه' (Allah suffices against his harm).
The phrase 'فانبذن عهده' indicates a clear break of allegiance.
2
Contrast the two rulings: kufr vs. other sins
The passage creates a sharp distinction: only kufr permits disobedience and removal. All other sins, including fisq, do not allow deposition. This is a foundational principle in Sunni political thought.
The author explicitly states 'بغير هذا لا يباح صرفه' - other than this, removal is not permitted.
3
Analyze the argument against removing a fasiq ruler
The last line argues that a ruler is not removed even if his description (justice) is removed through sin. This counters the view of some scholars who advocate removal for fisq.
The author uses the phrase 'وليس يعزل إن أزيل وصفه' to assert the permanence of rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ (المُبِينِ أي : ولا تخرج عن امتثال أمره الواضح الجاري على قواعد الشريعة وفي كلامه حذف الواو مع ما عطفت، والتقدير (عن أمره ونهيه كما أشار إليه الشارح، ولو حمل الأمر في النظم على الشأن لعم الأمرين جميعاً، فتجب طاعته على جميع الرعايا ظاهراً وباطناً ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولى الأمر منكم) [النساء: 59]، وهم العلماء والأمراء، ولقوله : «مَنْ أطاعَ أميري فقد أطاعني، ومَنْ عصى أميري فقد عصاني» (١) ، لكن لا يُطاع في الحرام (١)، والمكروه، وأما المباح فإن كان فيه مصلحة عامة للمسلمين وجب طاعته فيه، وإلا فلا ؛ فلو نادى بعدم شرب الدخان المعروف الآن وجبت عليهم طاعته؛ لأنَّ في إبطاله مصلحة عامةً ؛ إذ في تعاطيه خِسَّةٌ لذوي الهيآت ووجوه الناس، خصوصاً إذا كان في القهاوي، وقد وقع أنه أمر بترك الدخان في الأسواق والقهاوي، فيحرم الآن(٢). قوله : (إِلَّا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ أي : إلا إذا أمر بكفر فاطرحن بيعته جهراً، فإن لم تقدر على الجهر بذلك فاطرحها سِرا . وقوله : (فَاللَّهُ يَكْفِينَا أَذَاهُ وَحْدَهُ أي : فالله تعالى يكفينا أذى الإمام الذي أمر بالكفر وحده؛ إذ هو الذي ناصيته بقدرته (۳) قوله : بِغَيْرِ هَذَا لَا يُبَاحُ صَرْفُهُ أي : بغير هذا الكفر من جميع المعاصي لا يجوز خلعه عن الإمامة لا جهراً ولا سرا . وقوله : (وَلَيْسَ يُعْزَلْ إِنْ أُزِيلَ وَصْفُهُ) بسكون اللام من (يعزل) للوزن؛ أي: وليس يُعزل إذا وُلّي مستكملاً للشروط، ثم أزيل وصفه السابق؛ وهو العدالة بطرو الفسق، خلافاً لطائفة ذهبوا إلى أنه يُعزل بذلك.
And his saying: 'And do not deviate from his clear command' meaning: do not leave complying with his clear command that proceeds upon the rules of the law. And in his speech there is omission of the waw with what it coordinates, and the estimation is 'from his command and his prohibition' as the commentator indicated. And if 'command' in the poem is taken to mean 'authority', it would encompass both matters. So obedience to him is obligatory upon all subjects, outwardly and inwardly, due to the saying of the Most High: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' [an-Nisa: 59], and they are the scholars and the rulers, and due to his saying: 'Whoever obeys my commander has obeyed me, and whoever disobeys my commander has disobeyed me' (1). But he is not obeyed in the unlawful (1) and the disliked. As for the permissible, if in it there is a general benefit for the Muslims, obedience to him in it is obligatory, otherwise not. So if he were to issue a call to abstain from the now well-known tobacco, obedience to him would be obligatory upon them, because in its abolition is a general benefit; for in its consumption is baseness for people of dignity and faces of people, especially if it is in coffeehouses. And it has occurred that he ordered the abandonment of tobacco in markets and coffeehouses, so it is now forbidden (2). His saying: 'Except with kufr, then throw away his covenant' meaning: except if he commands kufr, then discard his allegiance openly, and if you are unable to do so openly, then discard it secretly. And his saying: 'For Allah suffices us against his harm alone' meaning: Allah the Most High suffices us against the harm of the imam who commanded kufr alone; for He is the one whose forelock is in His power (3). His saying: 'Other than this, his removal is not permitted' meaning: other than this kufr, from all sins, it is not permissible to depose him from the imamate, neither openly nor secretly. And his saying: 'And he is not removed if his description is removed' with sukun on the lam of 'yuzal' for the meter; meaning: he is not removed if he was appointed complete in the conditions, then his previous description was removed—that is, justice by the occurrence of sin—contrary to a group who held that he is removed thereby.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on lines regarding obedience to the ruler. The author explains each line sequentially. First, he explains 'ولا تزغ عن أمره المبين' meaning do not deviate from the ruler's clear command, emphasizing that obedience is obligatory on all subjects based on Quran and hadith. He notes that obedience is not required in haram or makruh, and for mubah, it becomes obligatory if there is public benefit, citing an example of banning tobacco. Then, the exception 'إلا بكفر فانبذن عهده' clarifies that if the ruler commands kufr, one must reject his allegiance openly or secretly if unable. Next, 'فالله يكفينا أذاه وحده' reassures that Allah will protect from the harm of such a ruler. Then, 'بغير هذا لا يباح صرفه' emphasizes that for any sin other than kufr, deposing the ruler is not permitted. Finally, 'وليس يعزل إن أزيل وصفه' argues that even if the ruler becomes unjust (fasiq), he is not removed from office, contrary to some scholars. The passage thus establishes the Sunni position on obedience to rulers: obligatory except in clear kufr, and permanent tenure even after sin.

Key Terms

بيعة
فسق
عزل
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that a ruler who commits major sins should be deposed immediately.
Correction: The passage states that only kufr justifies removal; other sins, including fisq, do not permit deposition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is it permissible to depose a ruler?
A) When he commits any major sin
B) When he commands kufr
C) When he becomes unjust
D) When he neglects public welfare

Key Takeaway
Only kufr breaks the contract with a ruler; a sinful or unjust ruler is not to be removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there commands from any authority in your life—family, work, community—that you would consider kufr? If so, have you truly examined where the line of kufr lies, or do you use the word too loosely?
?
The lesson says Allah 'suffices us against his harm alone.' Is there a ruler, teacher, or parent whose harm you fear so much that you forget that Allah holds their forelock? What would it look like to truly trust that promise today?

Commanding Good and Forbidding Evil: Obligation and Levels
The author defines commanding good and forbidding evil, states its obligation, evidences, and three levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil, its evidence, and the three levels of forbidding wrongdoing.

Core Idea
Commanding good and forbidding evil is a collective duty, even for sinners.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains a central social duty in Islam: enjoining good and forbidding evil. The author clarifies the definitions, the rulings, and provides evidence from the Quran and Sunnah. He also addresses the common misconception that only sinless individuals may perform this duty, and outlines the three levels of intervention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the author's rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: definitions, rulings, evidence, levels.
The passage is dense with legal details.
2
Identify the definitions and rulings
Find how the author defines 'ma'ruf' and 'munkar' and what rulings (recommended vs obligatory) apply to each. Note the concept of collective obligation (fard kifaya).
The ruling differs based on whether the action is obligatory/recommended or forbidden/disliked.
3
Study the evidence and the three levels
Review the Quranic verse and the hadith cited. Then focus on the three levels of forbidding: by hand, by tongue, and by heart. Understand when each level applies.
The hadith shows a progression from strongest to weakest intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَأْمُرْ بِعُرْفٍ وَاجْتَنِبْ نَمِيمَهُ وَغِيْبَةٌ وَخَصْلَةٌ ذَمِيمَة) قوله : (وَأْمُرْ بِعُرْفٍ) أي: وانه عن منكر، ففيه حذف الواو مع ما عطفت، وإنما ترك المصنف النهي عن المنكر لاستلزام الأمر له . والعُرف بضم العين: لغةٌ في المعروف، وهو ما عرفه الشرع، وهو الواجب والمندوب ؛ والمُنكر : ما أنكره الشرع، وهو الحرام والمكروه؛ فيندب الأمر بالمندوب والنهي عن المكروه، ويجب الأمر بالواجب والنهي عن الحرام وجوباً كفائيا، فإذا قام به البعض سقط الطلب عن الباقين، وهو فوري إجماعاً . ولا يختص وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر بمن لا يرتكب مثله، بل من رأى منكراً، وهو يرتكب مثله فعليه أن ينهى عنه ؛ ولهذا قال إمام الحرمين : (يجب على متعاطي الكأس أن ينكر على الجُلاس)، وقال الغزالي : يجب على من زنى بامرأة أَمْرُها بستر وجهها عنه. والدليل على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر: الكتاب والسنة والإجماع. أما الكتاب فكقوله تعالى : وَلتَكُن مِّنكُمْ أُمَّةٌ يَدْعُونَ إِلَى الْخَيْرِ وَيَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَيَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ ﴾ [آل عِمرَان : ١٠٤] . وأما السنةُ فكحديث أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : مَنْ رأى منكم منكراً فليغيره بيده، فإن لم يستطع فبلسانه، فإن لم يستطع فبقلبه وذلك أضعفُ الإيمان » (١) ، أي : أقل ثمراته (۲) لدلالته على عدم انتظامه وإلا فلا يُكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها، فمراتب الإنكار ثلاث : أقواها : أن يغير بيده، ويليها التغيير بالقول، وأضعفها الإنكار بالقلب؛ بأن يكرهه بقلبه ولا يرضى به .
His saying: 'And command what is recognized and avoid his gossip and backbiting and a blameworthy trait' His saying: 'And command what is recognized' meaning: and forbid the disapproved, so in it there is omission of the waw with what it coordinates, and the author only omitted forbidding the disapproved because commanding implies it. And 'al-urf' with damm on the 'ayn is a variant of 'al-maruf', which is what the Law recognizes, and it is the obligatory and the recommended. And 'al-munkar' is what the Law disapproves, and it is the forbidden and the disliked. So it is recommended to command the recommended and forbid the disliked, and it is obligatory to command the obligatory and forbid the forbidden as a collective obligation; if some undertake it, the requirement falls from the rest, and it is immediate by consensus. And the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is not specific to one who does not commit the like of it; rather, whoever sees a disapproved act, while he commits its like, he must forbid it. For this reason Imam al-Haramayn said: 'It is obligatory upon the one who drinks a cup to forbid the companion,' and al-Ghazali said: 'It is obligatory upon one who has committed zina with a woman to command her to cover her face from him.' And the evidence for the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book, it is like the saying of the Most High: 'Let there be from you a nation inviting to good, commanding the recognized, and forbidding the disapproved' [Al Imran: 104]. As for the Sunnah, it is like the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, say: Whoever among you sees a disapproved act, let him change it with his hand; if he is unable, then with his tongue; if he is unable, then with his heart, and that is the weakest of faith' (1), meaning the least of its fruits (2) because it indicates its lack of order—otherwise Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. So the levels of forbidding are three: the strongest is that he changes it with his hand, next is change by speech, and the weakest is forbidding with the heart, by hating it in his heart and not being pleased with it.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line demanding commanding good and forbidding evil. The author first explains the line, clarifying that 'urf' is synonymous with 'maruf', defined as what the Law recognizes (obligatory and recommended), while 'munkar' is what the Law disapproves (forbidden and disliked). He then states the rulings: it is recommended (mandub) to command the recommended and forbid the disliked, and obligatory (wajib kifaya) to command the obligatory and forbid the forbidden—an obligation that is immediate and falls upon the community as a whole. He emphasizes that this duty is not conditional upon being sinless oneself; even a sinner must forbid evil to others, citing Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali. He then provides evidence from the Quran (Surah Al Imran: 104) and the Sunnah (the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri). Finally, he explains the three levels of forbidding: by hand (strongest), by tongue (middle), and by heart (weakest), with the last being a mere inner dislike indicating weak faith.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمعروف
النهي عن المنكر
معروف
منكر
فريضة كفائية
إنكار بالقلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only sinless people can command good and forbid evil.
Correction: The duty applies to everyone, including sinners, as long as they see the evil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on forbidding the disapproved?
A) It is recommended (mandub)
B) It is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn)
C) It is a collective obligation (fard kifaya)
D) It is forbidden for sinners

Key Takeaway
Commanding good and forbidding evil is a collective obligation with three levels of intervention, and it remains obligatory even for those who commit sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen a sin committed openly, yet you felt nothing in your heart—no anger, no grief. What does that indifference say about the strength of your faith, and what will you do today to revive that inner objection?

?
The strongest level of forbidding is with the hand, but you have the power to change something in your home or community—why have you not used it? Is it fear, laziness, or arrogance? Repent and act now, before the sin becomes normalized.

Avoiding Backbiting (Namimah) – Definition and Six Duties
Definition of namimah, six duties, and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of backbiting (namimah), the six duties incumbent upon one who hears it, and the exceptional cases where revealing information becomes permissible or obligatory.

Core Idea
Backbiting is revealing what is disliked, not just false reports; hearing it imposes six duties on the listener.

In this passage, the author explains the serious sin of backbiting (namimah) and clarifies what exactly constitutes it. Beyond the simple definition of transmitting speech to cause harm, scholars expand it to any disclosure of disliked information. Furthermore, the one who hears a backbiting report has specific responsibilities—six duties—that protect him from sharing in the sin. The author also discusses when revealing such information may be allowed or even required for legitimate reasons.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of namimah
Read the opening lines defining namimah, including the broader definition by al-Ghazali and al-Nawawi. Note that truthfulness does not exempt from sin.
The core idea is that backbiting is about revealing what is disliked, not just about falsehood.
2
Learn the six duties of the listener
Go through each of the six duties listed by al-Nawawi. They form a practical guide for handling hearsay.
The duties are ordered: not believing, forbidding and advising, hating the backbiter, not thinking ill, not spying, and not repeating.
3
Recognize the exceptions for legitimate need
Read the concluding section that allows revealing information when it is for advice or preventing harm. The exception transforms the act from backbiting to advice or obligation.
This shows that intention and need can change the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَاجْتَنِبْ نَمِيمَهُ) أي: انفرّ منها وتباعد عنها، والأمر في ذلك للوجوب العيني. والنميمة : نقل كلام الناس بعضهم إلى بعض على وجه الإفساد بينهم؛ كقوله : فلان يقول فيك كذا، لكن قال أبو حامد الغزالي وليست النميمة مختصة بذلك؛ بل حدها كشف ما يكره كشفه ؛ سواء كان الكشف بالقول أو بالكتابة أو بالرمز أو نحوها، وسواء كان المنقول من الأعمال أو من الأحوال، وسواء كان عيباً أو غيره. قال النووي : فحقيقة النميمة : إفشاء السرّ، وهتك الستر عما يكره كشفه . قال : وكل من حُمِلتُ إليه نميمةٌ لزمه ستة أمور : الأول: ألا يصدِّقَه ؛ لأن النمام فاسقٌ، والفاسق مردود الخبر. الثاني : أن ينهاه عن ذلك، وينصحه. الثالثُ : أن يُبغضه فإنه بغيض عند الله ، ويجب بغض من أبغضه الله تعالى. الرابع : ألا يظنَّ بالمنقول عنه السوء؛ لقوله تعالى: اجْتَنِبُوا كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الظَّنِّ إِنَّ بَعْضَ الظَّنِّ إِثْمٌ [الحجرات: 12]. الخامس : ألا يحمله ما حكى له على التجسس والبحث عن تحقيق ذلك، قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَجَسَّسُوا﴾ [الحجرات: 12] السادس : ألا يحكي تميمة عنه فيقول : فلان حكى لى كذا، فيصير بذلك نماماً . والنميمة محرمة بالإجماع، والمذاهب متفقة على أنها كبيرة، لحديث «الصحيحين»: لا يدخل الجنةَ نمام»، وفي رواية مسلم: «فَتَّات» (1) ؛ بتاءين أولاهما مشددة؛ أي : نمام، من (قَتَّ الحديثَ) نمَّه والمراد : لا يدخلها مع السابقين، إلا إن غُفر له . وكلُّ ذلك ما لم تدعُ الحاجة إليها، وإلا جازت ؛ لأنها حينئذ ليست نميمة، بل نصيحة، كما إذا أخبرك شخص بأن فلاناً يريد البطش بمالك أو بأهلك أو نحو ذلك ؛ لتكون على حذر، فليس ذلك بحرام لما فيه من دفع المفاسد، وقد يكون بعضه واجباً، كما إذا تيقن وقوع ذلك لو لم يخبرك بهذا الخبر، وقد يكون بعضه مستحبا، كما إذا شك في ذلك؛ ذكره النووي. أفاده المصنف في «شرحه».
His saying: (And avoid his backbiting) meaning: flee from it and stay away from it, and the command regarding that is for personal obligation. And backbiting (al-namimah) is: transmitting people's speech to one another with the intent of causing corruption between them; like saying: So-and-so says such-and-such about you. But Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said: Backbiting is not exclusive to that; rather its definition is revealing what one dislikes to be revealed; whether the revealing is by speech or writing or gesture or the like, and whether the transmitted matter is actions or states, and whether it is a fault or otherwise. Al-Nawawi said: The reality of backbiting is divulging secrets and tearing the veil from what one dislikes to be revealed. He said: And whoever has a backbiting report brought to him must do six things: First: not to believe it, because the backbiter (namam) is a sinner, and the sinner's report is rejected. Second: to forbid him from that and advise him. Third: to hate him, for he is hateful to Allah, and one must hate whom Allah hates. Fourth: not to think ill of the person reported about, due to Allah's saying: 'Avoid much suspicion; indeed some suspicion is sin' [Hujurat:12]. Fifth: not to let what was told to him lead him to spy and investigate to verify that, as Allah said: 'And do not spy' [Hujurat:12]. Sixth: not to transmit the backbiting from him, saying: So-and-so told me such, thus becoming a backbiter himself. And backbiting is forbidden by consensus, and the schools agree that it is a major sin, due to the hadith in the 'Sahihain': 'A backbiter will not enter Paradise,' and in Muslim's narration: 'Fattat' with two taa's the first shadda, i.e., backbiter, from qatta al-hadith, meaning he spread it, and the meaning is: he will not enter it with the foremost, unless he is forgiven. And all that applies as long as there is no need for it; otherwise it is allowed, for then it is not backbiting but advice, as when someone informs you that so-and-so intends to attack your property or your family or the like, so that you are cautious; that is not forbidden because of warding off harm, and sometimes it may be obligatory, as when he is certain that it will occur if he does not inform you with this report, and sometimes it may be recommended, as when he doubts that; mentioned by al-Nawawi. Explained by the author in his commentary.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines the sin of backbiting (namimah) and outlines the duties of the person who hears such a report. It begins by explaining the command in the poem to avoid backbiting, emphasizing that it is a personal obligation. The definition of namimah is given as transmitting people's speech to cause corruption, but al-Ghazali expands it to any revelation of what is disliked, by any means. Al-Nawawi further defines it as divulging secrets and removing the cover from what is disliked. Then the author lists six duties upon the hearer: (1) not to believe the backbiter because he is sinful and his report is unreliable; (2) to forbid and advise the backbiter; (3) to hate the backbiter for Allah's sake; (4) not to think ill of the person spoken about, citing the Qur'anic prohibition against suspicion; (5) not to spy to verify the report, also citing the Qur'an; (6) not to repeat the backbiting, lest one become a backbiter. The passage then affirms the consensus that backbiting is a major sin, citing the hadith that a backbiter will not enter Paradise (except with forgiveness). Finally, it notes exceptions: if there is a legitimate need, such as warning someone of harm, it is not backbiting but advice, and may be obligatory or recommended depending on the certainty of harm.

Key Terms
نميمة
فاسق
إفشاء السر
ستة أمور
النمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a report is true, it is not backbiting.
Correction: Truth does not exempt; backbiting is defined by revealing what is disliked, regardless of truth. Only a legitimate need (like warning of harm) makes it permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third duty of the person who hears backbiting?
A) To forbid the backbiter and advise him
B) To hate the backbiter for the sake of Allah
C) Not to transmit the backbiting further
D) To verify the report before believing it

Key Takeaway
Backbiting is divulging disliked information, and the listener has six duties to avoid complicity; exceptions apply for genuine need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a report about someone's fault, does your heart immediately lean toward believing it? How can you train yourself to pause and recall that the backbiter is a fasiq whose news is rejected—and what will you do the next time you are tempted to accept gossip?
?
Think of a time you repeated something negative about someone absent, even if it was true. Did you consider that you might have become a backbiter? What specific step from the six duties could you have applied—and what concrete action will you take now to make amends with that person or with Allah?
?
If you know of a harmful situation (like someone planning to wrong another), speaking up is not backbiting but advice. Are you using this excuse too loosely to justify gossip about those you dislike? How will you judge your own intention the next time you share information about someone, and what inner check will you perform before speaking?

The Definition and Boundaries of Ghibah (Backbiting)
Defines ghibah and clarifies its scope beyond mere speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ghibah, its scope beyond speech, and the key exceptions that make criticism permissible advice.

Core Idea
Backbiting is not limited to lies; even true negative statements about a Muslim are forbidden unless for a valid purpose.

Ghibah is often misunderstood as only false speech, but the scholars define it broadly: mentioning something your brother dislikes, even if true. This passage from the commentary clarifies the boundaries, including when criticism becomes permissible advice and when vague references still count as backbiting.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where ghibah is defined as 'mentioning your brother in a way he dislikes, even if true.' Note that truthfulness does not exempt it from sin.
The definition includes 'even if in his presence', though some distinguish it as buhtan.
2
Expand the scope beyond speech
The passage states that ghibah is not limited to speech but includes any indication of deficiency—by word, writing, or gesture (eye, hand, head, etc.)—regarding any aspect of a person's life.
Examples: body, religion, family, profession, clothing.
3
Recognize the warnings
The author warns against the common rationalization 'it's just the truth,' which can lead to deeming the sin permissible (istihlal) and even disbelief. Also note the distinction between ghibah (absent) and buhtan (present).
4
Identify the exceptions
Learn when criticism is not ghibah: when it is advice (nasiha) to prevent harm or error, or when the person mentioned is not identifiable to the listener. But if the listener can identify from context, it remains forbidden.
Authors citing errors of others are permitted if the intent is to warn against being misled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَغِيْبَةٌ) أي : واجتنب غيبةً، والأمر فيه للوجوب العيني، كما في سابقه. والغيبة بكسر الغين ذكرك أخاك بما يكره، ولو بما فيه ولو بحضوره، لكن ظاهر المادة يؤيد ما قيل : مِنْ أن ما في الحضور لا يُسمَّى غيبةً، بل بُهتاناً، وإذا ذكره بما ليس فيه فقد زاد إثمُ الكذب . ومن الضلال قول بعض العامة : ليس هذا غيبة إنما هو إخبار بالواقع، فربما جَرَّه ذلك لكفر الاستحلال، والعياذ بالله . وليست الغيبة مختصة بالذِّكْرِ، بل ضابطها كلُّ ما أفهمْتَ به غيرك نقصان مسلم بلفظك أو كتابتك أو أشرت إليه بعينك أو يدك أو رأسك أو نحو ذلك، سواء كان ذلك في بدنه أو دينه أو دنياه أو ولده أو والده أو زوجته أو خادمه أو حرفته أو لونه أو مركوبه أو عمامته أو ثوبه أو غير ذلك، مما يتعلق به . ومن ذلك قول المصنفين في كتبهم : قال فلان كذا، وهو غلط أو خطأ أو نحو ذلك، فهو حرام إلا إن أرادوا بيان غلطه أو خطئه لئلا يُقلَّد؛ لأن ذلك نصيحة لا غيبة . وقولهم : قال مصنف، أو قال قوم أو جماعة كذا وهو غلط أو خطأ، أو نحو ذلك .. ليس غيبة ؛ لأن الغيبة لا تكون إلا في إنسان معين أو جماعة معينين . وقولك : فعل كذا بعض الناس أو بعض الفقهاء أو مَنْ يدعي العلم أو بعض المفتين، أو نحو ذلك .. غيبة محرَّمةٌ إذا كان المخاطب يفهمه بعينه، وقضية ذلك أنك إذا ذكرت شخصاً تعرفه أنت دون المخاطب لا يكون غيبة، ويشكل عليه حرمة الغيبة في الخلوة دون حضور أحدٍ، وكذا بالقلب فقط، فإنها بالقلب محرمة، ومحلُّ ذلك في غير من شاهَدَ، وأما من شاهد فيعذر في الاعتقاد حينئذ، نعم ينبغي أن يحمله على أنه تاب. كهي باللسان.
His saying: (And backbiting/ghibah) meaning: and avoid backbiting, and the command for it is for personal obligation, as in the previous. And backbiting (al-ghibah) – with kasra on the ghayn – is your mentioning your brother in a way he dislikes, even if it is true, even if in his presence, but the apparent meaning of the word supports what has been said: that what is said in his presence is not called ghibah, but rather slander (buhtan), and if he mentions him with what is not in him, then he has added the sin of lying. And it is misguidance from some common people to say: 'This is not backbiting, it is just telling the truth,' for perhaps that may lead him to disbelief due to deeming it permissible, Allah forbid. And backbiting is not exclusive to speech; rather its definition is everything by which you make another understand a deficiency of a Muslim, by your word or writing or pointing with your eye or hand or head or the like, whether that is in his body, religion, worldly affairs, child, father, wife, servant, profession, color, mount, turban, garment, or other things related to him. And among that is the saying of authors in their books: 'So-and-so said such, and it is wrong or mistaken or the like' – that is forbidden unless they intend to clarify his error or mistake so that he not be imitated, because that is advice not backbiting. And their saying: 'An author said,' or 'a group or community said such, and it is wrong or mistaken or the like' – that is not backbiting, because backbiting only applies to a specific person or specific group. And your saying: 'So-and-so from the people or some jurists or those claiming knowledge or some muftis did such' or the like – it is forbidden backbiting if the listener understands him specifically. And the consequence of that is that if you mention a person whom you know but the listener does not know, it is not backbiting. But there is a problem regarding the prohibition of backbiting in private without anyone present, and similarly only in the heart, for it is forbidden in the heart. And that applies to one who did not witness [the act]; as for one who witnessed, then he is excused in his belief then, but he should assume that he repented. It is like the same as with the tongue.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines the sin of backbiting (ghibah) and clarifies its boundaries. It begins with the command to avoid backbiting, again a personal obligation. The definition is given: mentioning your brother in a way he dislikes, even if true, even in his presence. However, there is a nuance that what is said in his presence is sometimes called buhtan (slander) rather than ghibah, and if false, adds the sin of lying. The author warns against the common person's rationalization that 'it's just the truth,' which can lead to deeming the sin permissible to the point of disbelief. Then the scope is broadened: ghibah is not limited to speech but includes any indication of a Muslim's deficiency, by word, writing, or gesture, regarding any aspect (body, religion, worldly matters, family, etc.). It then addresses authors who criticize others in their books: it is forbidden unless the intent is to prevent imitation of error, which is advice. Speaking about an unspecified author or group is not backbiting because it lacks specificity. However, if the listener can identify the person from a vague description, it becomes forbidden. A subtle point: if you mention someone the listener does not know, it may not be backbiting, but the problem of private heart-sins remains. The passage concludes by noting that one who actually witnessed a sin is excused from assuming continued sin, though he should assume repentance. The overall point is that ghibah is defined broadly, and its prohibition is strict.

بهتان
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghibah is only false speech; if it is true, it is not backbiting.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that ghibah includes true statements: 'your mentioning your brother in a way he dislikes, even if it is true.' Truthfulness does not exempt it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered backbiting?
A) Writing a letter that mentions a friend's fault truthfully
B) Warning someone that a known person intends to harm them
C) Pointing at a person's missing tooth in their absence
D) Saying 'some people are careless about prayer' where the listener knows exactly who you mean

Key Takeaway
Ghibah is a broad sin covering any true or false communication that humiliates a Muslim, with exceptions only for valid advice or unspecified references.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you said something negative about someone to a friend, justifying it as 'but it's true.' Were you seeking advice or venting? What change will you make starting today to avoid falling into ghibah again?
?
The passage says even a glance or a gesture can be ghibah. In the past week, how many times did you subtly mock or belittle someone with just a look or a shrug? Will you seek forgiveness from Allah and from that person?
?
You now know that even thinking ill of someone in your heart is forbidden. Is there a person you've been silently judging or harboring resentment toward? Will you sincerely assume good of them and pray for them today?

Definition and Scope of Ghibah
Classifies ghibah by specificity and target's status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scenarios of backbiting based on the specificity of the person to speaker and listener, and the differing rulings for Muslims, dhimmis, and harbis.

Core Idea
Ghibah's prohibition depends on who hears and who is spoken about.

The author begins by introducing a scholarly opinion that distinguishes whether the person backbitten is specifically known or ambiguous to the participants. This sets the stage for understanding when ghibah is forbidden. The passage also addresses the common question: can we backbite a non-Muslim?

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the first sentence and note the three scenarios based on whether the person is specific or ambiguous to speaker and listener.
The ruling changes with each condition.
2
Understand the ruling for each
For each scenario, determine if backbiting is forbidden or permissible according to the opinion cited.
3
Distinguish between harbi and dhimmi
The passage then discusses backbiting against non-Muslims. Note the difference between an enemy combatant and a protected non-Muslim.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the hadith specification
Why do the hadiths specify Muslims? The passage explains that this is due to Muslim being primary audience; it does not mean non-Muslims are fair game.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر بعضهم أنه إن كان معيناً عند الذاكر والسامع حرمت، وإن كان مبهماً عندهما جازت، وإن كان مُبهماً عند السامع دون الذاكر حرمت على الذاكر دون السامع.
وذكر الأخ في التعريف السابق لذكره في بعض الأحاديث، وقد أخذ به جمع، وقالوا : لا غيبة في الكافر . والحقُّ أنه إن كان حربيًّا فلا غيبةً فيه، وإن كان ذميًّا حرمت غيبته، وتخصيص المسلم بالذكر في الأحاديث الشريفة.
وحكم الغيبة التحريم بالإجماع، وفي الكتاب العزيز : أَيُحِبُّ أَحَدُكُمْ أَن يَأْكُل لَحْمَ أَخِيهِ مَبْنا ...) [الحجرات: (۱۲) الآية، وفي هذه الآية تنفير شديد؛ لأنها اشتملت على خمسة أمور؛ وهي كونه لحماً وميتاً ونيئاً ومن آدمي وأخ.
وفي سنن أبي داود والترمذي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: قلت للنبي : حسبك من صفية كذا وكذا - تعني قصيرة - فقال : لقد قلتِ كلمةٌ لو مرجَتْ بماء البحر لمزجته (۱)
قال النووي: معنى مزجته خالطته بحيث يتغير بها طعمه أو ريحه لشدّة نتنها وقبحها. وهذا الحديث من أعظم الزواجر عن الغيبة وأعمها .
وقد اختلف العلماء في مرتبتها من التحريم ؛ فقال القرطبي من المالكية : إنها كبيرة بلا خلاف يعني في المذهب، وإليه ذهب كثير من الشافعية، وذكر صاحب «العدة» عنهم أنها صغيرةٌ، وأقره عليه الرافعي ومن تبعه لعموم البلوى بها، فقلَّ مَنْ يسلم منها، وفي التعليل نظر لا يخفى؛ لأنَّ ذلك لا يقتضي كونها من الصغائر، والذي جزم به ابن حجر الهيتمي في شرح الشمائل أن غيبة العالم وحامل القرآن كبيرة، وغيبةً غيرهما صغيرة، وهو المعتمد.
وكما يحرم على المغتاب ذكر الغيبة يحرم على السامع استماعها وإقرارها ؛ فيجب على كل من سمع إنساناً يذكر غيبة محرمة أن ينهاه إن لم يخف ضرراً ظاهراً.
وقد ورد: مَنْ رَدَّ غِيبةَ مسلم ردَّ اللهُ النارَ عن وجهه يومَ القيامةِ» (۱)
فإن لم يستطع باليد ولا باللسان فارق ذلك المجلس، ولا يخلص الإنكار بحـ الظاهر.
فإن قال بلسانه اسكت، وهو يشتهي بقلبه استمراره، فذلك نفاق، كما قاله الغزالي، فلا بد من كراهته بقلبه (۲) ، وربما الحق مجلس الغيبة بمظان الإجابة، فيقول : الله يلطف بنا وبفلان فعل كذا وكذا ؛ ومن ذلك غيبة المتفقهين والمتعبدين، فيُقال لأحدهم: كيف حال فلان؟ فيقول: الله يصلحنا والله يغفر لنا، الله يصلحه، نسأل الله العافية، الله يتوب علينا وما أشبه ذلك مما يفهم منه تنقيصه، فكل ذلك غِيبةٌ مُحرَّمةٌ، وكذلك إذا قال: فلان ما له حيلة، كلنا نفعل ذلك.
And some of them mentioned that if it is specific to the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; and if it is ambiguous to both, it is permissible; and if it is ambiguous to the listener but not the speaker, it is forbidden for the speaker, not the listener. And the brother mentioned in the previous definition because it occurs in some hadiths, and a group adopted it, and they said: there is no ghibah against the disbeliever. But the truth is that if he is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah regarding him; and if he is a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), his ghibah is forbidden, and the specification of the Muslim in the noble hadiths is because of that. The ruling of ghibah is prohibition by consensus, and in the Mighty Book: 'Would any of you like to eat the flesh of his brother dead...' [al-Hujurat 49:12] the verse, and in this verse there is severe deterrence because it comprises five matters: that it is flesh, dead, raw, from a human, and from a brother. And in the Sunan of Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that she said: I said to the Prophet: 'It is enough for you of Safiyyah such and such' – meaning short – so he said: 'You have said a word which, if it were mixed with seawater, it would change it (1).' Al-Nawawi said: The meaning of 'change it' is that it would mix with it such that its taste or smell changes due to its intense foulness and ugliness. This hadith is among the greatest deterrents against ghibah and the most comprehensive. And the scholars differed regarding its rank of prohibition: al-Qurtubi of the Maliki school said it is a major sin without dispute, meaning in the school, and many of the Shafi'is went to that view; and the author of al-'Uddah mentioned from them that it is a minor sin, and al-Rafi'i and those who followed him agreed with it due to the widespread affliction with it, as few are safe from it. And in the reasoning there is a consideration that is not hidden, because that does not necessitate it being among the minor sins. And what Ibn Hajar al-Haytami decisively stated in Sharh al-Shama'il is that ghibah of a scholar and a carrier of the Quran is a major sin, and ghibah of others is a minor sin, and it is the relied-upon view. And just as it is forbidden for the one who backbites to mention ghibah, it is forbidden for the listener to hear it and affirm it; so it is obligatory upon everyone who hears a person mention forbidden ghibah to forbid him, if he does not fear apparent harm. And it has been reported: 'Whoever repels ghibah against a Muslim, Allah will repel the fire from his face on the Day of Resurrection' (1). If he is unable with his hand or tongue, let him leave that gathering, and the disapproval is not effective outwardly. If he says with his tongue 'be silent' while his heart desires its continuation, then that is hypocrisy, as al-Ghazali said, so it is necessary to dislike it in his heart (2). And perhaps one might join the gathering of ghibah where supplication is expected, saying: 'May Allah be kind to us and to so-and-so' while they are doing such and such. And among that is the ghibah of the students of fiqh and the devout. Some says to one of them: 'How is so-and-so?' and he says: 'May Allah correct us, may Allah forgive us, may Allah correct him, we ask Allah for well-being, may Allah grant us repentance' and similar things from which understood as a diminishment of him. So all of that is forbidden ghibah. And likewise if he says: 'So-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of ghibah (backbiting) in detail. It begins by presenting a nuanced opinion: if the person backbitten is specifically known to both the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; if ambiguous to both, it is permissible; if ambiguous only to the listener, it is forbidden for the speaker but not the listener. The author then discusses the view that there is no ghibah against a disbeliever, clarifying truth: if the disbeliever is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah; if a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), it is forbidden. The specification of Muslims in hadiths is because they are the primary audience. The ruling by consensus is prohibition, supported by Quran 49:12, which compares it to eating the flesh of a dead brother, and the author counts five reasons from the verse making it repulsive. A hadith from Aisha is cited where the Prophet rebukes her for calling Safiyyah short, stating such words could pollute seawater – showing extreme seriousness. Al-Nawawi's commentary explains the metaphor. Then the passage presents scholarly disagreement over whether ghibah is a major or minor sin: al-Qurtubi and many Shafi'is consider it major; others consider it minor due to widespread prevalence. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami distinguishes: ghibah of a scholar or Quran carrier is major; of others, minor – this is the relied-upon view. The passage then addresses the listener's duty: just as speaking it is forbidden, listening and affirming it is also forbidden. The listener must forbid the speaker unless fearing harm. If unable, he must leave the gathering. If he says 'stop' but desires continuation, that is hypocrisy; genuine inward dislike is required. The author warns about subtle backbiting among students of fiqh and ascetics, where one uses pious phrases that imply criticism, such as 'may Allah correct him' etc. All such expressions are forbidden if understood as diminishment. Finally, saying 'so-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that' is also forbidden ghibah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Backbiting is always forbidden regardless of who knows or the target.
Correction: The passage shows backbiting can be permissible if the person is ambiguous to both, and against a harbi it is not considered ghibah.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of backbiting a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) according to the passage?
A) It is permissible because ghibah is only forbidden against Muslims.
B) It is forbidden.
C) It is permissible only if the dhimmi is present.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of ghibah depends on the listener's knowledge and the target's protected status; it is not absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The lesson shows that ghibah is not always forbidden—it depends on context. Have you ever used a loophole like 'I'm not naming names' to feel better about speaking ill of someone? How does this passage expose the condition of your heart? Will you repent and change your habits starting today?
