Evidence for the Prohibition of Ghibah
Presents evidence for prohibition: consensus, Quran, hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence for the prohibition of ghibah: consensus, Quran, and hadith, including the severe warning in the verse and the Prophet's rebuke to Aisha.

Core Idea
Ghibah is condemned by Quran and Sunnah with intense imagery.

After establishing the definition, the author now presents the ruling of ghibah as prohibition by consensus and supports it with powerful evidence from the Quran and a hadith. The Quranic verse compares backbiting to eating the flesh of one's dead brother, and a hadith from Aisha shows the gravity of even a minor comment.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Notice the author says ghibah is forbidden by consensus. This sets the legal weight.
2
Analyze the Quranic verse
Read the verse excerpt and the author's commentary on the five repulsive aspects: flesh, dead, raw, human, brother.
This is meant to disgust the reader.
3
Study the hadith from Aisha
Read the story where the Prophet rebukes Aisha for calling Safiyyah short. Note how even a small comment can be serious.
Al-Nawawi's commentary explains the metaphor.
4
Reflect on the deterrent effect
The author calls this hadith one of the greatest deterrents. Think about why.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وذكر الأخ في التعريف السابق لذكره في بعض الأحاديث، وقد أخذ به جمع، وقالوا : لا غيبة في الكافر . والحقُّ أنه إن كان حربيًّا فلا غيبةً فيه، وإن كان ذميًّا حرمت غيبته، وتخصيص المسلم بالذكر في الأحاديث الشريفة.
وحكم الغيبة التحريم بالإجماع، وفي الكتاب العزيز : أَيُحِبُّ أَحَدُكُمْ أَن يَأْكُل لَحْمَ أَخِيهِ مَبْنا ...) [الحجرات: (۱۲) الآية، وفي هذه الآية تنفير شديد؛ لأنها اشتملت على خمسة أمور؛ وهي كونه لحماً وميتاً ونيئاً ومن آدمي وأخ.
وفي سنن أبي داود والترمذي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: قلت للنبي : حسبك من صفية كذا وكذا - تعني قصيرة - فقال : لقد قلتِ كلمةٌ لو مرجَتْ بماء البحر لمزجته (۱)
قال النووي: معنى مزجته خالطته بحيث يتغير بها طعمه أو ريحه لشدّة نتنها وقبحها. وهذا الحديث من أعظم الزواجر عن الغيبة وأعمها .
وقد اختلف العلماء في مرتبتها من التحريم ؛ فقال القرطبي من المالكية : إنها كبيرة بلا خلاف يعني في المذهب، وإليه ذهب كثير من الشافعية، وذكر صاحب «العدة» عنهم أنها صغيرةٌ، وأقره عليه الرافعي ومن تبعه لعموم البلوى بها، فقلَّ مَنْ يسلم منها، وفي التعليل نظر لا يخفى؛ لأنَّ ذلك لا يقتضي كونها من الصغائر، والذي جزم به ابن حجر الهيتمي في شرح الشمائل أن غيبة العالم وحامل القرآن كبيرة، وغيبةً غيرهما صغيرة، وهو المعتمد.
وكما يحرم على المغتاب ذكر الغيبة يحرم على السامع استماعها وإقرارها ؛ فيجب على كل من سمع إنساناً يذكر غيبة محرمة أن ينهاه إن لم يخف ضرراً ظاهراً.
وقد ورد: مَنْ رَدَّ غِيبةَ مسلم ردَّ اللهُ النارَ عن وجهه يومَ القيامةِ» (۱)
فإن لم يستطع باليد ولا باللسان فارق ذلك المجلس، ولا يخلص الإنكار بحـ الظاهر.
فإن قال بلسانه اسكت، وهو يشتهي بقلبه استمراره، فذلك نفاق، كما قاله الغزالي، فلا بد من كراهته بقلبه (۲) ، وربما الحق مجلس الغيبة بمظان الإجابة، فيقول : الله يلطف بنا وبفلان فعل كذا وكذا ؛ ومن ذلك غيبة المتفقهين والمتعبدين، فيُقال لأحدهم: كيف حال فلان؟ فيقول: الله يصلحنا والله يغفر لنا، الله يصلحه، نسأل الله العافية، الله يتوب علينا وما أشبه ذلك مما يفهم منه تنقيصه، فكل ذلك غِيبةٌ مُحرَّمةٌ، وكذلك إذا قال: فلان ما له حيلة، كلنا نفعل ذلك.
And some of them mentioned that if it is specific to the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; and if it is ambiguous to both, it is permissible; and if it is ambiguous to the listener but not the speaker, it is forbidden for the speaker, not the listener. And the brother mentioned in the previous definition because it occurs in some hadiths, and a group adopted it, and they said: there is no ghibah against the disbeliever. But the truth is that if he is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah regarding him; and if he is a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), his ghibah is forbidden, and the specification of the Muslim in the noble hadiths is because of that. The ruling of ghibah is prohibition by consensus, and in the Mighty Book: 'Would any of you like to eat the flesh of his brother dead...' [al-Hujurat 49:12] the verse, and in this verse there is severe deterrence because it comprises five matters: that it is flesh, dead, raw, from a human, and from a brother. And in the Sunan of Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that she said: I said to the Prophet: 'It is enough for you of Safiyyah such and such' – meaning short – so he said: 'You have said a word which, if it were mixed with seawater, it would change it (1).' Al-Nawawi said: The meaning of 'change it' is that it would mix with it such that its taste or smell changes due to its intense foulness and ugliness. This hadith is among the greatest deterrents against ghibah and the most comprehensive. And the scholars differed regarding its rank of prohibition: al-Qurtubi of the Maliki school said it is a major sin without dispute, meaning in the school, and many of the Shafi'is went to that view; and the author of al-'Uddah mentioned from them that it is a minor sin, and al-Rafi'i and those who followed him agreed with it due to the widespread affliction with it, as few are safe from it. And in the reasoning there is a consideration that is not hidden, because that does not necessitate it being among the minor sins. And what Ibn Hajar al-Haytami decisively stated in Sharh al-Shama'il is that ghibah of a scholar and a carrier of the Quran is a major sin, and ghibah of others is a minor sin, and it is the relied-upon view. And just as it is forbidden for the one who backbites to mention ghibah, it is forbidden for the listener to hear it and affirm it; so it is obligatory upon everyone who hears a person mention forbidden ghibah to forbid him, if he does not fear apparent harm. And it has been reported: 'Whoever repels ghibah against a Muslim, Allah will repel the fire from his face on the Day of Resurrection' (1). If he is unable with his hand or tongue, let him leave that gathering, and the disapproval is not effective outwardly. If he says with his tongue 'be silent' while his heart desires its continuation, then that is hypocrisy, as al-Ghazali said, so it is necessary to dislike it in his heart (2). And perhaps one might join the gathering of ghibah where supplication is expected, saying: 'May Allah be kind to us and to so-and-so' while they are doing such and such. And among that is the ghibah of the students of fiqh and the devout. Some says to one of them: 'How is so-and-so?' and he says: 'May Allah correct us, may Allah forgive us, may Allah correct him, we ask Allah for well-being, may Allah grant us repentance' and similar things from which understood as a diminishment of him. So all of that is forbidden ghibah. And likewise if he says: 'So-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of ghibah (backbiting) in detail. It begins by presenting a nuanced opinion: if the person backbitten is specifically known to both the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; if ambiguous to both, it is permissible; if ambiguous only to the listener, it is forbidden for the speaker but not the listener. The author then discusses the view that there is no ghibah against a disbeliever, clarifying truth: if the disbeliever is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah; if a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), it is forbidden. The specification of Muslims in hadiths is because they are the primary audience. The ruling by consensus is prohibition, supported by Quran 49:12, which compares it to eating the flesh of a dead brother, and the author counts five reasons from the verse making it repulsive. A hadith from Aisha is cited where the Prophet rebukes her for calling Safiyyah short, stating such words could pollute seawater – showing extreme seriousness. Al-Nawawi's commentary explains the metaphor. Then the passage presents scholarly disagreement over whether ghibah is a major or minor sin: al-Qurtubi and many Shafi'is consider it major; others consider it minor due to widespread prevalence. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami distinguishes: ghibah of a scholar or Quran carrier is major; of others, minor – this is the relied-upon view. The passage then addresses the listener's duty: just as speaking it is forbidden, listening and affirming it is also forbidden. The listener must forbid the speaker unless fearing harm. If unable, he must leave the gathering. If he says 'stop' but desires continuation, that is hypocrisy; genuine inward dislike is required. The author warns about subtle backbiting among students of fiqh and ascetics, where one uses pious phrases that imply criticism, such as 'may Allah correct him' etc. All such expressions are forbidden if understood as diminishment. Finally, saying 'so-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that' is also forbidden ghibah.

Key Terms
تحريم
إجماع
أكل لحم الميت
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many repulsive aspects does the author count in the Quranic verse about backbiting?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Ghibah is forbidden by consensus and condemned by Quran and hadith with the strongest imagery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you spoke about someone in their absence, and did you pause to imagine that you were consuming their dead flesh? Today, commit to catching yourself before such a word leaves your lips.
?
The Prophet said a single remark could pollute the sea. How many such remarks have you let out? What will you do to ensure your speech is pure from now on?
?
You know now that even true statements can be ghibah. Think of a person you have criticized behind their back. Will you seek their forgiveness? Write down their name and your plan to make amends.

Unseen Matters
intermediate
5 min
Is Ghibah a Major or Minor Sin?
Presents debate on sin rank and the relied-upon view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether ghibah is a major (kabirah) or minor (saghirah) sin and the relied-upon distinction based on the status of the person backbitten.

Core Idea
The gravity of backbiting depends on who is backbitten.

After establishing the prohibition, the author addresses a scholarly debate: is ghibah a major or minor sin? Some scholars consider it always major, others say minor due to its prevalence. The relied-upon view distinguishes based on the victim's religious standing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different positions
Read the passage and list the opinions: al-Qurtubi says major, others say minor, Ibn Hajar distinguishes.
2
Understand the reasoning
Notice why some consider it minor: widespread affliction makes it seem less severe. The author critiques this reasoning.
The author says 'في التعليل نظر' meaning the reasoning is questionable.
3
Focus on the relied-upon view
Ibn Hajar al-Haytami's view: ghibah of a scholar or Quran carrier is major; of others, minor. This is called 'al-mu'tamad'.
This is the view the author seems to endorse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر بعضهم أنه إن كان معيناً عند الذاكر والسامع حرمت، وإن كان مبهماً عندهما جازت، وإن كان مُبهماً عند السامع دون الذاكر حرمت على الذاكر دون السامع.
وذكر الأخ في التعريف السابق لذكره في بعض الأحاديث، وقد أخذ به جمع، وقالوا : لا غيبة في الكافر . والحقُّ أنه إن كان حربيًّا فلا غيبةً فيه، وإن كان ذميًّا حرمت غيبته، وتخصيص المسلم بالذكر في الأحاديث الشريفة.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All backbiting is equally sinful.
Correction: According to the relied-upon view, the sin's severity depends on the status of the person backbitten.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the passage, when is backbiting a major sin?
A) Always
B) When the person backbitten is a scholar or Quran carrier
C) Only when the backbiter is a scholar
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The severity of backbiting varies: it is a major sin when targeting scholars and Quran carriers; otherwise it is minor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about a scholar or someone who is known for their religious commitment, do you guard your tongue more carefully? Or do you treat them like everyone else? After this lesson, will you change how you speak about them?
?
Have you ever dismissed backbiting of a non-scholar as 'just a small sin'? What does that say about your concern for the harm you cause? Are you willing to repent and treat all backbiting seriously, even if the sin is minor?
?
If you learned that a Quran carrier in your community is being backbitten, would you defend them or join in? How can you actively protect the honor of those whom Allah has honored?

Unseen Matters

Duties of the Listener and Subtle Backbiting
Lists duties of listener and warns against disguised backbiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligations of the listener when hearing backbiting and the subtle forms of backbiting that often go unnoticed.

Core Idea
Listening to backbiting is as sinful as speaking it, and disguised criticism is still backbiting.

The author now addresses the responsibility of the listener: just as backbiting is forbidden to speak, it is forbidden to hear and affirm it. The listener must actively oppose it, and even subtle remarks that imply criticism are considered backbiting. This lesson helps identify hidden pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Read the listener's obligations
Note the sequence: forbid with hand/tongue, leave the gathering, dislike in heart. Hypocrisy if heart desires continuation.
2
Understand the hadith about repelling
The hadith promises that Allah will repel fire from the face of one who repels ghibah.
Great incentive to act.
3
Identify subtle forms of backbiting
The passage gives examples: using pious phrases like 'may Allah correct him' that imply fault, or saying 'he has no stratagem, we all do that'.
These are disguised criticisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وقد ورد: مَنْ رَدَّ غِيبةَ مسلم ردَّ اللهُ النارَ عن وجهه يومَ القيامةِ» (۱)
فإن لم يستطع باليد ولا باللسان فارق ذلك المجلس، ولا يخلص الإنكار بحـ الظاهر.
فإن قال بلسانه اسكت، وهو يشتهي بقلبه استمراره، فذلك نفاق، كما قاله الغزالي، فلا بد من كراهته بقلبه (۲) ، وربما الحق مجلس الغيبة بمظان الإجابة، فيقول : الله يلطف بنا وبفلان فعل كذا وكذا ؛ ومن ذلك غيبة المتفقهين والمتعبدين، فيُقال لأحدهم: كيف حال فلان؟ فيقول: الله يصلحنا والله يغفر لنا، الله يصلحه، نسأل الله العافية، الله يتوب علينا وما أشبه ذلك مما يفهم منه تنقيصه، فكل ذلك غِيبةٌ مُحرَّمةٌ، وكذلك إذا قال: فلان ما له حيلة، كلنا نفعل ذلك.
And some of them mentioned that if it is specific to the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; and if it is ambiguous to both, it is permissible; and if it is ambiguous to the listener but not the speaker, it is forbidden for the speaker, not the listener. And the brother mentioned in the previous definition because it occurs in some hadiths, and a group adopted it, and they said: there is no ghibah against the disbeliever. But the truth is that if he is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah regarding him; and if he is a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), his ghibah is forbidden, and the specification of the Muslim in the noble hadiths is because of that. The ruling of ghibah is prohibition by consensus, and in the Mighty Book: 'Would any of you like to eat the flesh of his brother dead...' [al-Hujurat 49:12] the verse, and in this verse there is severe deterrence because it comprises five matters: that it is flesh, dead, raw, from a human, and from a brother. And in the Sunan of Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that she said: I said to the Prophet: 'It is enough for you of Safiyyah such and such' – meaning short – so he said: 'You have said a word which, if it were mixed with seawater, it would change it (1).' Al-Nawawi said: The meaning of 'change it' is that it would mix with it such that its taste or smell changes due to its intense foulness and ugliness. This hadith is among the greatest deterrents against ghibah and the most comprehensive. And the scholars differed regarding its rank of prohibition: al-Qurtubi of the Maliki school said it is a major sin without dispute, meaning in the school, and many of the Shafi'is went to that view; and the author of al-'Uddah mentioned from them that it is a minor sin, and al-Rafi'i and those who followed him agreed with it due to the widespread affliction with it, as few are safe from it. And in the reasoning there is a consideration that is not hidden, because that does not necessitate it being among the minor sins. And what Ibn Hajar al-Haytami decisively stated in Sharh al-Shama'il is that ghibah of a scholar and a carrier of the Quran is a major sin, and ghibah of others is a minor sin, and it is the relied-upon view. And just as it is forbidden for the one who backbites to mention ghibah, it is forbidden for the listener to hear it and affirm it; so it is obligatory upon everyone who hears a person mention forbidden ghibah to forbid him, if he does not fear apparent harm. And it has been reported: 'Whoever repels ghibah against a Muslim, Allah will repel the fire from his face on the Day of Resurrection' (1). If he is unable with his hand or tongue, let him leave that gathering, and the disapproval is not effective outwardly. If he says with his tongue 'be silent' while his heart desires its continuation, then that is hypocrisy, as al-Ghazali said, so it is necessary to dislike it in his heart (2). And perhaps one might join the gathering of ghibah where supplication is expected, saying: 'May Allah be kind to us and to so-and-so' while they are doing such and such. And among that is the ghibah of the students of fiqh and the devout. Some says to one of them: 'How is so-and-so?' and he says: 'May Allah correct us, may Allah forgive us, may Allah correct him, we ask Allah for well-being, may Allah grant us repentance' and similar things from which understood as a diminishment of him. So all of that is forbidden ghibah. And likewise if he says: 'So-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of ghibah (backbiting) in detail. It begins by presenting a nuanced opinion: if the person backbitten is specifically known to both the speaker and listener, it is forbidden; if ambiguous to both, it is permissible; if ambiguous only to the listener, it is forbidden for the speaker but not the listener. The author then discusses the view that there is no ghibah against a disbeliever, clarifying truth: if the disbeliever is a harbi (enemy combatant), there is no ghibah; if a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), it is forbidden. The specification of Muslims in hadiths is because they are the primary audience. The ruling by consensus is prohibition, supported by Quran 49:12, which compares it to eating the flesh of a dead brother, and the author counts five reasons from the verse making it repulsive. A hadith from Aisha is cited where the Prophet rebukes her for calling Safiyyah short, stating such words could pollute seawater – showing extreme seriousness. Al-Nawawi's commentary explains the metaphor. Then the passage presents scholarly disagreement over whether ghibah is a major or minor sin: al-Qurtubi and many Shafi'is consider it major; others consider it minor due to widespread prevalence. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami distinguishes: ghibah of a scholar or Quran carrier is major; of others, minor – this is the relied-upon view. The passage then addresses the listener's duty: just as speaking it is forbidden, listening and affirming it is also forbidden. The listener must forbid the speaker unless fearing harm. If unable, he must leave the gathering. If he says 'stop' but desires continuation, that is hypocrisy; genuine inward dislike is required. The author warns about subtle backbiting among students of fiqh and ascetics, where one uses pious phrases that imply criticism, such as 'may Allah correct him' etc. All such expressions are forbidden if understood as diminishment. Finally, saying 'so-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that' is also forbidden ghibah.

Key Terms
سامع
إنكار
نفاق
غيبة مغلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only speaking backbiting is sinful; listening is harmless.
Correction: Listening and affirming backbiting is also forbidden; the listener must actively oppose.

Checkpoint
What should a listener do if he cannot forbid the backbiter with hand or tongue?
A) Stay silent and enjoy the conversation
B) Leave the gathering
C) Secretly agree
D) Change the subject

Key Takeaway
The listener must actively oppose backbiting; even subtle criticisms are backbiting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone being criticized or spoken about, what is the first reaction in your heart? Do you feel a secret pleasure or a genuine dislike? Would you be willing to leave the gathering if you cannot stop it?
?
Think of a time you used a phrase like 'May Allah forgive him' or 'We all have faults' in a way that hinted at someone's shortcomings. How can you purify your speech to avoid even subtle backbiting?
?
Is there a specific person or group you tend to criticize indirectly? What concrete step will you take this week to replace that habit with silence or defense of the absent?

Six Permissible Situations for Ghibah
Lists six permissible situations for ghibah with a poetic summary and explanation of the first four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six exceptional situations in which backbiting (ghibah) is permitted or even obligatory according to Islamic law.

Core Idea
Ghibah is forbidden, but allowed in six specific cases for legitimate benefit.

While ghibah (backbiting) is generally a major sin, scholars have recognized six situations where it is permissible or even obligatory for a greater good. This lesson presents these exceptions as organized by al-Jawjari in a poem, and explains the first four in detail: seeking redress, seeking help to change wrong, seeking a fatwa, and warning against harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic summary
Begin by reading the Arabic poem that lists all six permissible situations. Notice the key terms used.
The poem is composed by al-Jawjari in the al-Wafir meter.
2
Understand the first two cases
Focus on the first two cases: seeking redress (tazallum) and seeking help to change wrong (isti'anah). See the conditions mentioned.
Seeking help requires sincere intention to remove the wrong.
3
Study the third and fourth cases
Examine the third case (seeking a fatwa) and the fourth case (warning). Note that warning is only allowed if the person would not desist without mentioning faults.
Warning is for someone intending to associate with the person.
4
Identify the remaining two cases
The fifth and sixth cases (identifying and mentioning the sin of the open sinner) are named in the poem but not elaborated in this lesson. Reflect on why they might be permitted.
These are left for further study in other sources.
5
Reinforce through key takeaway
Conclude by affirming that these exceptions are for benefit and must be observed with proper conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الأحوال التي تجوزُ فيها الغيبةُ]
واعلم أن العلماء ذكروا أن الغيبة تُباح في أحوال للمصلحة، بل ربما وجبت؛ وتلك الأحوال ستة نظمها الجَوْجري - بجيمين على الصواب ـ في قوله: [من الوافر] مُنَظَمَةٌ كَأَمْثالِ الجَواهِرُ
لِسِتٌ غِيْبَةٌ كَرِّرٌ وَخُذْهَا تَظَلَّمْ وَاسْتَمِنْ وَاستَفْتِ حَذَّرْ وَعَرِّف واذْكُرَنْ فِسْقَ المُجَاهِرُ
فالأولُ : التظلم ؛ كأن يقول المظلوم لمن له الولاية كالقاضي : مثلاً . فلان ظلمني والثانيةُ : الاستعانة على تغيير المنكر ؛ كأن يقول لمن يرجو قدرته على إزالة المنكر : فلان يعمل كذا فأعني على منعه، بشرط أن يكون قصده التوصل إلى إزالة المنكر، فإن لم يقصد ذلك كان حراماً .
والثالثةُ : الاستفتاء؛ كأن يقول للمفتي ظلمني فلان، فهل له ذلك؟ وما طريقي في الخلاص منه ؟ .
:
والرابعةُ : التحذير ؛ كأن تذكر عيوب شخص لمن يريد الاجتماع عليه، إذا لم ينكف بدون ذكرها، وإلا حَرُمَ .
[The situations in which ghibah is permissible]
And know that the scholars mentioned that ghibah is permitted in situations for benefit, indeed sometimes obligatory. And those situations are six, which al-Jawjari – with two jims correctly – organized in his saying [in al-Wafir meter]: 'Arranged like pearls: six ghibahs – repeat and take them: seek redress, seek help, seek fatwa, warn, identify, and mention the sin of the one who is open.'
The first: seeking redress – as when the wronged person says to one in authority like the judge, for example, 'So-and-so wronged me.' The second: seeking help to change the wrong – as when one says to someone whose ability to remove the wrong he hopes for: 'So-and-so is doing such-and-such, so help me prevent him,' on the condition that his intention is to achieve removal of the wrong; if he does not intend that, it is forbidden.
The third: seeking a fatwa – as when one says to the mufti: 'So-and-so wronged me, does he have the right? And what is my way to be free from it?'
The fourth: warning – as when you mention the faults of a person to someone who intends to associate with him, if he will not desist without mentioning them; otherwise, it is forbidden.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage lists the exceptional situations where ghibah (backbiting) is permissible, even sometimes obligatory. The author states that scholars have allowed ghibah in certain cases for benefit, and enumerates six such cases, which al-Jawjari organized in a poem. Although the poem mentions all six, the extant passage explains only the first four. The first is 'tazallum' (seeking redress): the victim may mention the wrongdoer's action to a judge or authority to obtain justice. The second is 'isti'anah' (seeking help) to change a wrong: one may mention someone's wrongdoing to a person capable of stopping it, but only if the intention is to remove the evil; otherwise it is forbidden. The third is 'istifta'' (seeking a fatwa): the victim may describe the wrong to a mufti to ask about its permissibility and how to be free from it. The fourth is 'tahdhir' (warning): one may mention another's faults to someone who intends to associate with them, if the person would not be deterred without knowing those faults; if he would desist without them, mentioning them becomes forbidden. The fifth and sixth (ta'rif – identifying, and dhikr fisq al-mujahir – mentioning the sin of the open sinner) are named in the poem but not elaborated in this window.

Key Terms
تظلم
استعانة
استفتاء
تحذير
تعريف
مجاهر
غيبة
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ghibah (backbiting) is absolutely forbidden in all circumstances without exception.
Correction: The passage shows that ghibah is permitted in six specific situations where there is a greater benefit, and sometimes it may even become obligatory.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the permissible situations for ghibah mentioned in the passage?
A) Seeking redress against a wrongdoer
B) Seeking help to change a wrong
C) Warning someone about a potential business partner's faults
D) Spreading news of someone's misdeed for entertainment

Key Takeaway
Ghibah is generally forbidden but allowed in six exceptional cases — seeking redress, help, fatwa, warning, identifying, and mentioning the sin of an open sinner — each with specific conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are wronged, do you immediately seek redress by mentioning the person's fault to others who are not in authority, or do you restrict yourself to the permissible avenue of telling only those who can help? Reflect on a recent situation where you badmouthed someone who wronged you. Was it necessary? Could you have gone to a judge or person of authority instead? Commit today to change this habit.
?
The condition for 'seeking help' is that your intention is solely to remove the wrong. Examine your intentions when you complain about someone's wrongdoing to others. Is your goal truly to stop the harm, or do you derive satisfaction from belittling them? Be honest. What will you do differently?
?
When you warn others about someone's faults, do you check if the warning is actually needed, or do you assume it's okay? The passage says it is forbidden if the listener would desist without mentioning them. Have you ever spread negative information about someone that wasn't necessary? Repent and resolve to only speak when it is truly beneficial.

The Ruling and Response to Backbiting
The author explains the ruling on backbiting and the proper response to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on backbiting and the comprehensive Islamic response, including the need to hate it in the heart and avoid disguised forms.

Core Idea
Defending a Muslim's honor requires action, speech, and heartfelt hatred.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid addresses the serious sin of backbiting and outlines the proper response for a believer. Drawing on hadith and the words of al-Ghazali, it teaches that backbiting is not just about what we say, but also about what we tolerate in our hearts and how we avoid subtle forms of slander.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the structure: a hadith, followed by practical steps, then warnings about hypocrisy and disguised forms.
Pay attention to the conditional nature of the steps.
2
Identify the three levels of response
The passage lists three actions: stopping with the hand, stopping with the tongue, and leaving the gathering. Note that these are in order of ability.
If unable, leaving is obligatory.
3
Recognize the hidden sin: hypocrisy and disguised backbiting
The passage warns against outward denial while inwardly desiring the backbiting, calling it hypocrisy. It also gives examples of seemingly pious phrases that are actually backbiting.
This shows the importance of sincerity in the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ورد: من رد غيبة مسلم رد الله النار عن وجهه يوم القيامة فإن لم يستطع باليد ولا باللسان فارق ذلك المجلس، ولا يخلص الإنكار بظاهر. فإن قال بلسانه اسكت، وهو يشتهي بقلبه استمراره، فذلك نفاق، كما قاله الغزالي، فلا بد من كراهته بقلبه، وربما الحق مجلس الغيبة بمظان الإجابة، فيقول: الله يلطف بنا وبفلان فعل كذا وكذا؛ ومن ذلك غيبة المتفقهين والمتعبدين، فيقال لأحدهم: كيف حال فلان؟ فيقول: الله يصلحنا والله يغفر لنا، الله يصلحه، نسأل الله العافية، الله يتوب علينا وما أشبه ذلك مما يفهم منه تنقيصه، فكل ذلك غيبة محرمة، وكذلك إذا قال: فلان ما له حيلة، كلنا نفعل ذلك.
And it has been reported: 'Whoever defends his Muslim brother's backbiting, Allah will save his face from the fire on the Day of Resurrection.' If he is unable (to stop it) with his hand nor with his tongue, he should leave that gathering, and his denial is not sincere with (only) the outward. If he says with his tongue 'be quiet' while his heart desires its continuation, that is hypocrisy, as al-Ghazali said. So he must hate it in his heart. And perhaps he may join a gathering of backbiting that is a place for answering supplication, so he says: 'May Allah be kind to us and to so-and-so, he did such and such.' And among that is backbiting of jurists and worshippers: it is said to one of them: 'How is so-and-so?' He says: 'May Allah reform us and forgive us, may Allah reform him, we ask Allah for well-being, may Allah forgive us,' and similar things that imply belittlement of him. All of that is forbidden backbiting. Likewise if he says: 'So-and-so has no stratagem, we all do that.'
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines backbiting and explains the proper Islamic response to it. It begins by citing a hadith that encourages defending a Muslim's honor. Then it outlines the steps: if one cannot stop the backbiting physically (with the hand) or verbally (with the tongue), one must physically leave the gathering. A merely external denial while inwardly enjoying the backbiting is hypocrisy. The heart must hate it. The passage also warns of a common trick: when backbiting occurs in a gathering that might be a place of answered supplication, one might say something ambiguous like 'May Allah be kind to us and to so-and-so,' which implies censure. Similarly, when asked about a jurist or worshipper, one might respond with phrases like 'May Allah reform us' which imply imperfection in the person. All such disguised mentions are forbidden backbiting. The key point is that backbiting is not limited to direct insults; any statement that belittles someone is backbiting.

Key Terms
غيبة (backbiting)
نفاق (hypocrisy)
كراهة بقلب (hatred in the heart)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only direct insults are considered backbiting.
Correction: The passage shows that indirect, ambiguous phrases that imply belittlement are also forbidden backbiting.

Checkpoint
What should a person do if he is unable to stop backbiting with his hand or tongue?
A) Remain silent outwardly
B) Leave the gathering
C) Pretend to agree
D) Report the backbiter

Key Takeaway
Backbiting includes both explicit slander and subtle put-downs, and the believer must respond with action, speech, and a heart that truly disapproves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the last time you were in a gathering where someone was being spoken about negatively. Did your heart truly hate what was happening, or did it secretly enjoy the gossip? How will you confront that reality in yourself starting today?
?
You have likely used phrases like 'May Allah forgive us' or 'We all do that' when someone's fault was mentioned. Did you realize that could be backbiting? What will you do differently from now on to protect the honor of your brothers and sisters?
?
The hadith promises that defending a Muslim's honor will save your face from the fire. Are you willing to risk your own face in hell by staying silent or even participating in backbiting? What concrete action will you take this week to fulfill this defense?

Six Permissible Circumstances for Backbiting
The author enumerates six permissible cases of backbiting with examples and conditions, and refutes a related hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six situations in which backbiting is allowed, the conditions attached to each, and the status of the hadith 'No backbiting of a sinner.'

Core Idea
Backbiting is forbidden, but scholars exempt six cases for benefit, sometimes making it obligatory.

Scholars have outlined exceptions to the general prohibition of backbiting, where speaking about someone's fault serves a legitimate purpose. In this lesson, we examine the six permissible circumstances as classified by Imam al-Jawjari, each with its specific condition, and we also address a common misunderstood hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the poetic summary by al-Jawjari before the detailed explanation.
2
Identify the six categories
From the verse and subsequent explanation, list the six permissible circumstances in your own words.
Each circumstance has a name and a specific condition.
3
Understand each condition
For each of the six, note the example given and the condition that must be met to make it permissible.
Pay attention to the condition of intention in many cases.
4
Analyze the hadith response
The author addresses a hadith that seems to permit backbiting of sinners. Understand why it is not considered authentic and how it is restricted even if accepted.
This helps prevent a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن العلماء ذكروا أن الغيبة تباح في أحوال للمصلحة، بل ربما وجبت؛ وتلك الأحوال ستة نظمها الجوجري - بجيمين على الصواب - في قوله: [من الوافر] منظمة كأمثال الجواهر لست غيبة كرر وخذها تظلم واستمن واستفت حذر وعرف واذكرن فسق المجاهر فالأول: التظلم؛ كأن يقول المظلوم لمن له الولاية كالقاضي: مثلا . فلان ظلمني والثانية: الاستعانة على تغيير المنكر؛ كأن يقول لمن يرجو قدرته على إزالة المنكر: فلان يعمل كذا فأعني على منعه، بشرط أن يكون قصده التوصل إلى إزالة المنكر، فإن لم يقصد ذلك كان حراما . والثالثة: الاستفتاء؛ كأن يقول للمفتي ظلمني فلان، فهل له ذلك؟ وما طريقي في الخلاص منه؟ . والرابعة: التحذير؛ كأن تذكر عيوب شخص لمن يريد الاجتماع عليه، إذا لم ينكف بدون ذكرها، وإلا حرم . والخامسة: التعريف؛ كأن تقول: فلان الأعمش أو الأعرج، أو نحو ذلك، فيمن كان معروفا بذلك، بشرط أن يكون بنية التعريف فإن كان بقصد التنقيص حرم . والسادسة: أن يكون مجاهرا بفسقه؛ كالمجاهر بشرب الخمر وأخذ المكس وغير ذلك، فيجوز ذكره بما فسق به، لا بغيره من العيوب بشرط أن يقصد أن تبلغه لينزجر وحديث: لا غيبة في فاسق غير ثابت الصحة عند أهل العلم، ولو سلمت صحته وجب تقييده بما إذا اغتابه بما فسق به بعد مجاهرته به بالشرط المذكور.
And know that the scholars mentioned that backbiting is permissible in some situations for benefit, rather sometimes it is obligatory. Those situations are six, organized by al-Jawjari (with two jims correctly) in his verse: (al-wafir) 'A system like jewels, backbiting, repeat and take it: seek redress, seek help, seek fatwa, warn, identify, and mention the open sinner's sin.' The first: seeking redress; as when the wronged person says to the one in authority like the judge, for example: 'So-and-so wronged me.' The second: seeking help to change the evil; as when he says to someone he hopes can remove the evil: 'So-and-so does such-and-such, so help me prevent him,' on condition that his intention is to remove the evil; if he does not intend that, it is forbidden. The third: seeking a fatwa; as when he says to the mufti: 'So-and-so wronged me, does he have the right to that? And what is my way to be rid of it?' The fourth: warning; as when you mention the faults of a person to someone who intends to associate with him, if he cannot be deterred without mentioning them; otherwise it is forbidden. The fifth: identification; as when you say: 'So-and-so the bleary-eyed or the lame,' or the like, about someone known by that, on condition that it is for identification; if it is intended to belittle, it is forbidden. The sixth: that one is open in his sin; like one who openly drinks alcohol and takes illegal taxes and so on. It is permissible to mention him by what he sinned with, not other faults, on condition that he intends that the news reaches him so that he might desist. And the hadith: 'There is no backbiting of a sinner' is not authentically established according to the scholars, and even if its authenticity were accepted, it must be restricted to when he backbites him only about the sin that he commits openly, with the aforementioned condition.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage classifies the permissible cases of backbiting. It opens by stating that scholars have exempted backbiting when there is a legitimate benefit, sometimes even making it obligatory. The author then presents a poetic summary by al-Jawjari listing six cases: (1) Seeking justice from an authority; (2) Seeking help to stop an evil; (3) Asking a mufti for a legal opinion; (4) Warning someone about a potential associate; (5) Identifying a person by a physical defect known to all; (6) Mentioning the sins of someone who is openly sinful (al-mujahir). Each is explained with an example and condition. After listing, the author addresses the famous hadith "There is no backbiting of a sinner" and states that it is not authentic, and even if accepted, it would be restricted to backbiting about the openly committed sin only, provided one intends to reform the person.

Key Terms
تظلم
استعانة
استفتاء
تحذير
تعريف
مجاهرة
لا غيبة في فاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think backbiting a sinner is always allowed based on the hadith 'No backbiting of a sinner'.
Correction: The passage shows this hadith is not authentic, and even if accepted, it is restricted to backbiting only about the openly committed sin, with the intention of reform.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six permissible circumstances for backbiting according to this passage?
A) Seeking redress
B) Warning a friend about a potential spouse
C) Praising someone's good deeds
D) Identifying someone by a known physical defect

Key Takeaway
Backbiting is only permissible in six specific scenarios with conditions; the hadith allowing backbiting of a sinner is not authentic and even then is restricted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have spoken about someone's fault to another, were you truly seeking reform or was there a hidden satisfaction in exposing their weakness? How can you train your heart to check its intention before every word?
?
Think of a time you used the excuse of 'warning others' to share someone's sin. Were you more concerned about the listener's benefit or your own sense of moral superiority? Will you commit to only speaking if the benefit is clear and your intention is pure?
?
The condition for seeking help to change an evil requires your intention to be solely to remove the evil. Look back at a recent complaint you made about someone: was your intention entirely for reform, or did you also seek sympathy or validation? What accountability can you place on yourself to prevent slipping into gossip?

Repentance from Backbiting and Seeking Forgiveness
The author discusses the conditions for repentance from backbiting and seeking forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for valid repentance from backbiting, including the necessity of specifying the backbiting when seeking forgiveness, and the spiritual consequences that backbiting has on the backbiter.

Core Idea
Repentance from backbiting requires both turning to Allah and seeking specific forgiveness from the wronged person.

This passage delves into the nuanced rules of repentance from backbiting, a sin that involves both a wrong against Allah and a wrong against another person. The author, a Shafi'i scholar, explains the conditions under which one can be absolved from the human right, including the requirement to specify the exact words spoken. The lesson also highlights a powerful motivation to avoid backbiting: the harmful consequences that rebound upon the backbiter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the distinction between the internal act and the violation of the person's honor.
2
Identify the conditions for repentance
Find the conditions mentioned: when the victim knows vs. does not know, and the difference between seeking forgiveness and praying for them.
Also note the Shafi'i and Maliki differences on specification.
3
Understand the spiritual motivation
Read the part about the transfer of good deeds and sins, and Ibn al-Mubarak's comment. Reflect on how this can deter backbiting.
Consider the wisdom of focusing on one's own faults.
4
Apply the knowledge
Answer the checkpoint question to test your understanding of the Shafi'i view on seeking forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتوبة تنفع في الغيبة من حيث الإقدام عليها . وأما من حيث الوقوع في حرمة من هي له فلا بد فيها مع التوبة من طلب عفو صاحبها عنه إذا بلغته، وإذا لم تبلغه كفى الاستغفار له، وإن بلغته بعد ذلك بلغته ممحوة، ولا يصح إبراء صاحبها مع الجهل بما قاله، كأن يقول له: أنا قلت في كلاما فسامحني منه، بل لا بد من التعيين على الأصح من وجهين عندنا معاشر الشافعية، كأن يقول له: قلت في حقك كذا وكذا عند فلان وفلان فسامحني منه، ويكفي الإبراء مع الجهل عند المالكية كما هو ثاني الوجهين عندنا. ومما يعين على ترك الغيبة شهود أن ضررها عائد على النفس؛ فإنه ورد أنه تؤخذ له حسنات المغتاب لمن اغتابه، وتطرح عليه سيئاته، وعن ابن المبارك: (لو كنت مغتابا .. لاغتبت والدي؛ لأنهما أحق بحسناتي)؛ فالعاقل من اشتغل بعيوب نفسه.
Repentance is beneficial with regard to backbiting in terms of the act itself. As for the aspect of violating the sacredness of the one against whom it was done, it is necessary along with repentance to seek the forgiveness of its owner if he learns of it. If he does not learn of it, asking forgiveness for him suffices. If he learns of it after that, it reaches him as erased. And the absolution of the owner is not valid along with ignorance of what was said, as if he says to him: 'I said something about you, so forgive me for it,' rather specification is necessary according to the more correct of two views among us, the Shafi'is, as if he says: 'I said such and such about you in the presence of so-and-so and so-and-so, so forgive me for it.' And absolution with ignorance is sufficient according to the Malikis, as it is the second of two views among us. And among what helps to abandon backbiting is the awareness that its harm returns upon oneself. For it has been reported that the good deeds of the backbiter are taken and given to the one he backbit, and his sins are thrown upon him. And from Ibn al-Mubarak: 'If I were to backbite anyone, I would backbite my parents, for they have more right to my good deeds.' So the intelligent person occupies himself with his own faults.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage addresses repentance from backbiting. It distinguishes between the internal act of backbiting (the sin against Allah) and the external violation of another's honor (the sin against the person). For the sin against Allah, sincere repentance is sufficient. For the human right, one must also seek the person's forgiveness if the backbiting has reached him. If it has not reached him, it is enough to pray for his forgiveness. If he learns of it later, it will be considered erased. The passage then details the conditions for seeking forgiveness: it must be specific about what was said and in whose presence; a general apology is not sufficient according to the Shafi'i school. The Malikis allow a general apology. Finally, the passage encourages abandoning backbiting by reminding that its harm returns to the backbiter, as good deeds are transferred to the victim and sins are transferred to the backbiter, citing Ibn al-Mubarak's comment about preferring to backbite one's parents because they are more deserving of good deeds.

Key Terms
توبة (repentance)
إبراء (absolution)
استغفار (seeking forgiveness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general apology without details is enough to clear the sin of backbiting.
Correction: According to the Shafi'i school, the backbiter must specify the exact words and context when seeking forgiveness, unless the victim does not know.

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i view presented, what is required when seeking forgiveness from the backbitten person?
A) A general apology without specifics
B) Specifying the exact words and context
C) Simply saying 'forgive me'
D) Praying for the person without contacting him

Key Takeaway
Repentance from backbiting requires both sincere repentance to Allah and, if the victim knows, a specific apology detailing what was said; otherwise, general prayer for forgiveness suffices, but the harm of backbiting ultimately returns to the backbiter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you backbite, do you genuinely consider that your good deeds are being transferred to the person you spoke about? How does the thought of losing your hard-earned rewards change your attitude toward speaking about others?
?
If you have backbitten someone who knows what you said, have you specifically apologized to them detailing the words you used? If not, what holds you back, and when will you do it?
?
Ibn al-Mubarak said he would only backbite his parents because they deserve his good deeds the most. Who in your life would you least want to give your good deeds to? Why do you still backbite them?

What Is Envy? Defining Hasad and Distinguishing Ghibtah
Defines hasad and contrasts with ghibtah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hasad (envy) precisely and distinguish it from ghibtah (praiseworthy ambition).

Core Idea
Hasad is wishing for another's blessing to vanish; ghibtah is wishing for the same blessing.

Before we can understand why envy is forbidden, we must first know what it is. The author begins by giving a precise definition of hasad and contrasting it with a related but praiseworthy concept, ghibtah. This distinction is crucial for correctly identifying the spiritual disease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the key terms hasad and ghibtah.
Pay attention to the definition: tamannī zawāli ni‘mati al-ghayr.
2
Identify the definition of hasad
Find the exact wording for the definition of hasad. What does it mean to wish for removal of blessing?
3
Understand the difference with ghibtah
Contrast hasad with ghibtah. Ghibtah is wishing for a similar blessing without wanting it to be taken away.
The hadith about 'no hasad except in two' is mentioned.
4
Reflect on the moral seriousness
Why is hasad called 'the basest of the base'? Think about what it means to sell one's hereafter for another's world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَدَاءِ الحَسَدِ) أي : داء هو الحسد ; فالإضافة للبيان، هذا إن أريد الداء المعنوي، فإن أُريد الداء الحسي كان من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه; أي: الحسد الشبيه بالداء، وهو تمني زوال نعمة الغير ; سواء تمناها لنفسه أو لا، بأن تمنى انتقالها عن غيره لغيره، وهذا أخس الأخساء; لأنه باع آخرته بدنيا غيره ; بخلاف ما إذا تمنى مثل نعمة الغير، فإنه غِبْطَةٌ محمودة في الخير، كما ورد : لا حسد إلا في اثنتين ... الحديث . و دليل تحريمه الكتاب والسنة والإجماعُ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِن شَرِّ حَاسِدٍ إِذَا حَسَدَ﴾ [الفلق]، وشره كثير، فمنه ما هو غير مكتسب، وهو إصابة العين ومنه : ما هو مكتسب; كسعيه في تعطيل الخير عنه، وتنقيصه عند الناس، وربما دعا عليه أو بطش به، إلى غير ذلك. وقال : إياكم والحسد; فإنَّ الحسد يأكل الحسنات، كما تأكل النار الحطب أو العُشْبَ. ودواء الحسد النظر للوعيد مع أنه إساءة أدب مع الله تعالى; كأنه لا يُسلَّمُ له حكمه، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من المتقارب] أَلا قُلْ لِمَنْ باتَ لي حاسداً : أَتَدْرِي عَلَى مَنْ أَسَأْتَ الأَدَبْ؟ اَسَأتَ عَلَى اللهِ فِي فِعْلِهِ كَأَنَّكَ لَمْ تَرْضَ لِيْ مَا وَهَبْ فَكَانَ جَزَاؤُكَ أَنْ خَصَّنِي وَسَدَّ عَلَيْكَ طَرِيقَ الطَّلَبْ ومن الحكمة : الحسود لا يَسودُ ; أي: كثير الحسد لا تحصل له سيادة. ومن كلام أبي حنيفة رضي الله تعالى عنه : [من البسيط] إِنْ يَحْسُدُونِي فَإِنِّي غَيْرُ لَائِمِهِمْ قَبْلِي مِنَ النَّاسِ أَهْلُ الفَضْلِ قَدْ حُسِدُوا فَدَامَ لِيْ وَلَهُمْ مَا بِيْ ومَا بِهِمُ ومَاتَ أَكْثَرُنَا فَيْظاً بِما يَجِدُ أنَا الَّذِي يَجِدُونِي فِي صُدُورِهِم لَا أَرْتَقِيْ صَدَراً مِنْها وَلَا أَرِدُ ويُروى أنَّ إبليس قال لسيدنا نوح عليه الصلاة والسلام: (خذ خمساً، قال: لا أصدِّقُك، فأوحى الله إليه أن صَدِّقُهُ، فقال : قل; فقال : إياك والكبر، فإني إنما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه بالكبر، وإياك والحسد، فإن قابيل قتل أخاه هابيل بالحسد، وإياك والطمع، فإن آدم ما أورثه الله ما أورثه إلا بالطمع، وإياك والحرص، فإن حواء ما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه إلا بالحرص، وإياك وطول الأمل، فإنهما ما وقعا فيما وقعا فيه إلا بطول الأمل).
His statement: (and the disease of envy) means: a disease that is envy; so the addition is for clarification, this if what is meant is the figurative disease; but if what is meant is the physical disease, it is of the type of addition of the simile to the compared; i.e., envy is similar to a disease. And it is wishing for the removal of the blessing of another; whether he wishes it for himself or not, meaning he wishes its transfer from him to another. And this is the basest of the base; because he sold his hereafter for the world of another; unlike the case where he wishes for the like of the blessing of another, for that is a praiseworthy envy (ghibtah) in good, as it is reported: 'There is no envy except in two...' the hadith. And the proof of its prohibition is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. He said exalted: 'And from the evil of the envier when he envies' (Al-Falaq). And its evil is much. Some of it is not acquired, and that is the evil eye. And some of it is acquired, like his striving to nullify good from him, and diminish him in the eyes of people, and perhaps he prays against him or attacks him, and so on. And he (the Prophet) said: 'Beware of envy, for envy eats up good deeds just as fire eats up wood or grass.' And the cure for envy is to look at the threat (of punishment), and also that it is bad conduct towards Allah the Exalted; as if he does not submit to His decree. Therefore some said: [al-Mutaqarib meter] 'Oh say to the one who spent the night envying me: Do you know against whom you have committed bad manners? You acted badly towards Allah in His action, as if you were not pleased with what He granted me. So your punishment was that He favored me and blocked you from the way of seeking.' And from the wisdom: the envious does not become a master; i.e., a person who is much envious does not attain leadership. And from the sayings of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him: [al-Basit meter] 'If they envy me, I do not blame them; before me, among people, the virtuous have been envied. So may what I have and what they have remain for me and for them, and most of us died in rage at what they feel. I am the one whom they find in their hearts; I neither ascend to a high place from them nor do I go down.' And it is narrated that Iblis said to our master Nuh, peace be upon him: 'Take five. He said: I will not believe you. So Allah revealed to him that he should believe him. So he said: Say. He said: Beware of pride, for I only fell into what I fell into because of pride. Beware of envy, for Qabil killed his brother Habil because of envy. Beware of greed, for Adam did not inherit what he inherited except through greed. Beware of covetousness, for Hawwa only fell into what she fell into because of covetousness. Beware of prolonged hope, for they both only fell into what they fell into because of prolonged hope.'
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines and explains envy (hasad). It begins by clarifying the phrase 'the disease of envy' as a figurative disease, then gives the precise definition: wishing for the removal of a blessing from another, whether one wants it for oneself or not. It contrasts this with ghibtah, which is praiseworthy longing for a similar blessing. The author then provides evidence for the prohibition of envy from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. He explains that the evil of envy manifests in two forms: non-acquired (the evil eye) and acquired (actively harming the envied person). The cure for envy is twofold: contemplating the divine threat and recognizing that envy is bad manners towards Allah, as if one rejects His decree. The passage includes a poem illustrating this, along with wisdom sayings and a story where Iblis warns Prophet Nuh about five destructive traits: pride, envy, greed, covetousness, and prolonged hope. Each part builds the case that envy is a severe spiritual disease requiring treatment.

Key Terms
الحسد
الغبطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think hasad is simply disliking someone's success.
Correction: Hasad is specifically wishing for the removal of a blessing, not just disliking success. Wishing for a similar blessing (without removal) is ghibtah, which can be praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the precise definition of hasad?
A) Disliking someone's achievements.
B) Wishing for the removal of a blessing from another.
C) Wishing for the same blessing as another.
D) Feeling jealous of someone's wealth.

Key Takeaway
Hasad is the wish for another's blessing to vanish; this is the opposite of ghibtah, which is the desire for similar good without malice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone else succeed, what is the first movement in your heart? Is it joy for them, or do you catch yourself wishing their blessing would diminish? Sift through your feelings honestly: where do you stand between hasad and ghibtah?
?
The passage says that hasad makes you sell your hereafter for another person's worldly blessing. Which worldly blessing of another person are you most tempted to envy? Will you, right now, make a du'a for that person's blessing to increase, and ask Allah to purify your heart?
?
You have likely felt ghibtah for someone's wealth or knowledge. But have you ever acted on that ghibtah by striving to acquire similar good? If not, what is holding you back—is it laziness, or is there a trace of hasad in your heart that prefers their loss over your gain?

Why Is Envy Forbidden? Proofs and Two Forms of Harm
Provides proof of prohibition and classifies harms of hasad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence for the prohibition of hasad and recognize its two types of harm: the evil eye and active harm.

Core Idea
Hasad is prohibited by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus; its harm comes in two forms: non-acquired (evil eye) and acquired (active deeds).

Having defined hasad, the author now turns to its prohibition. He cites the Quranic verse, the Prophet's warning, and the consensus of scholars. He then explains that the harm caused by hasad can be either supernatural (the evil eye, which is not under direct control) or intentional actions to harm the envied person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs
Find the three sources of evidence mentioned: Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.
The verse from Surah Al-Falaq is quoted.
2
Understand the two categories of harm
Note the division: harm that is non-acquired (the evil eye) and harm that is acquired (active seeking to nullify blessings).
3
Reflect on the gravity
Consider why the author calls the acquired harm 'much evil' and lists specific actions like slander and physical attack.
4
Connect to the definition
How does this classification relate to the definition of hasad? Is the evil eye itself a form of hasad?

Source Text

قوله : (وَدَاءِ الحَسَدِ) أي : داء هو الحسد ; فالإضافة للبيان، هذا إن أريد الداء المعنوي، فإن أُريد الداء الحسي كان من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه; أي: الحسد الشبيه بالداء، وهو تمني زوال نعمة الغير ; سواء تمناها لنفسه أو لا، بأن تمنى انتقالها عن غيره لغيره، وهذا أخس الأخساء; لأنه باع آخرته بدنيا غيره ; بخلاف ما إذا تمنى مثل نعمة الغير، فإنه غِبْطَةٌ محمودة في الخير، كما ورد : لا حسد إلا في اثنتين ... الحديث . و دليل تحريمه الكتاب والسنة والإجماعُ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِن شَرِّ حَاسِدٍ إِذَا حَسَدَ﴾ [الفلق]، وشره كثير، فمنه ما هو غير مكتسب، وهو إصابة العين ومنه : ما هو مكتسب; كسعيه في تعطيل الخير عنه، وتنقيصه عند الناس، وربما دعا عليه أو بطش به، إلى غير ذلك. وقال : إياكم والحسد; فإنَّ الحسد يأكل الحسنات، كما تأكل النار الحطب أو العُشْبَ. ودواء الحسد النظر للوعيد مع أنه إساءة أدب مع الله تعالى; كأنه لا يُسلَّمُ له حكمه، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من المتقارب] أَلا قُلْ لِمَنْ باتَ لي حاسداً : أَتَدْرِي عَلَى مَنْ أَسَأْتَ الأَدَبْ؟ اَسَأتَ عَلَى اللهِ فِي فِعْلِهِ كَأَنَّكَ لَمْ تَرْضَ لِيْ مَا وَهَبْ فَكَانَ جَزَاؤُكَ أَنْ خَصَّنِي وَسَدَّ عَلَيْكَ طَرِيقَ الطَّلَبْ ومن الحكمة : الحسود لا يَسودُ ; أي: كثير الحسد لا تحصل له سيادة. ومن كلام أبي حنيفة رضي الله تعالى عنه : [من البسيط] إِنْ يَحْسُدُونِي فَإِنِّي غَيْرُ لَائِمِهِمْ قَبْلِي مِنَ النَّاسِ أَهْلُ الفَضْلِ قَدْ حُسِدُوا فَدَامَ لِيْ وَلَهُمْ مَا بِيْ ومَا بِهِمُ ومَاتَ أَكْثَرُنَا فَيْظاً بِما يَجِدُ أنَا الَّذِي يَجِدُونِي فِي صُدُورِهِم لَا أَرْتَقِيْ صَدَراً مِنْها وَلَا أَرِدُ ويُروى أنَّ إبليس قال لسيدنا نوح عليه الصلاة والسلام: (خذ خمساً، قال: لا أصدِّقُك، فأوحى الله إليه أن صَدِّقُهُ، فقال : قل; فقال : إياك والكبر، فإني إنما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه بالكبر، وإياك والحسد، فإن قابيل قتل أخاه هابيل بالحسد، وإياك والطمع، فإن آدم ما أورثه الله ما أورثه إلا بالطمع، وإياك والحرص، فإن حواء ما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه إلا بالحرص، وإياك وطول الأمل، فإنهما ما وقعا فيما وقعا فيه إلا بطول الأمل).
His statement: (and the disease of envy) means: a disease that is envy; so the addition is for clarification, this if what is meant is the figurative disease; but if what is meant is the physical disease, it is of the type of addition of the simile to the compared; i.e., envy is similar to a disease. And it is wishing for the removal of the blessing of another; whether he wishes it for himself or not, meaning he wishes its transfer from him to another. And this is the basest of the base; because he sold his hereafter for the world of another; unlike the case where he wishes for the like of the blessing of another, for that is a praiseworthy envy (ghibtah) in good, as it is reported: 'There is no envy except in two...' the hadith. And the proof of its prohibition is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. He said exalted: 'And from the evil of the envier when he envies' (Al-Falaq). And its evil is much. Some of it is not acquired, and that is the evil eye. And some of it is acquired, like his striving to nullify good from him, and diminish him in the eyes of people, and perhaps he prays against him or attacks him, and so on. And he (the Prophet) said: 'Beware of envy, for envy eats up good deeds just as fire eats up wood or grass.' And the cure for envy is to look at the threat (of punishment), and also that it is bad conduct towards Allah the Exalted; as if he does not submit to His decree. Therefore some said: [al-Mutaqarib meter] 'Oh say to the one who spent the night envying me: Do you know against whom you have committed bad manners? You acted badly towards Allah in His action, as if you were not pleased with what He granted me. So your punishment was that He favored me and blocked you from the way of seeking.' And from the wisdom: the envious does not become a master; i.e., a person who is much envious does not attain leadership. And from the sayings of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him: [al-Basit meter] 'If they envy me, I do not blame them; before me, among people, the virtuous have been envied. So may what I have and what they have remain for me and for them, and most of us died in rage at what they feel. I am the one whom they find in their hearts; I neither ascend to a high place from them nor do I go down.' And it is narrated that Iblis said to our master Nuh, peace be upon him: 'Take five. He said: I will not believe you. So Allah revealed to him that he should believe him. So he said: Say. He said: Beware of pride, for I only fell into what I fell into because of pride. Beware of envy, for Qabil killed his brother Habil because of envy. Beware of greed, for Adam did not inherit what he inherited except through greed. Beware of covetousness, for Hawwa only fell into what she fell into because of covetousness. Beware of prolonged hope, for they both only fell into what they fell into because of prolonged hope.'
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines and explains envy (hasad). It begins by clarifying the phrase 'the disease of envy' as a figurative disease, then gives the precise definition: wishing for the removal of a blessing from another, whether one wants it for oneself or not. It contrasts this with ghibtah, which is praiseworthy longing for a similar blessing. The author then provides evidence for the prohibition of envy from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. He explains that the evil of envy manifests in two forms: non-acquired (the evil eye) and acquired (actively harming the envied person). The cure for envy is twofold: contemplating the divine threat and recognizing that envy is bad manners towards Allah, as if one rejects His decree. The passage includes a poem illustrating this, along with wisdom sayings and a story where Iblis warns Prophet Nuh about five destructive traits: pride, envy, greed, covetousness, and prolonged hope. Each part builds the case that envy is a severe spiritual disease requiring treatment.

Key Terms
الحسد
العين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hasad is only a sin if you act on it, but the evil eye is also a harmful consequence of envy even without action.
Correction: The passage includes the evil eye as a form of harm from hasad, showing that even unintentional harm can occur, though it is not acquired.

Checkpoint
What are the two categories of harm from hasad mentioned in the passage?
A) Spiritual and physical
B) Non-acquired (evil eye) and acquired (active harm)
C) Major and minor
D) Internal and external

Key Takeaway
Hasad is prohibited by all major sources of Islamic law, and its harm can be either the evil eye or deliberate actions against the envied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific person you have envied. Have you ever said something that diminished them in the eyes of others? This lesson teaches that acquired harm from hasad includes slander. Will you repent and commit to guarding your tongue from now on?
?
The evil eye can harm even without intention. Do you regularly recite the morning and evening adhkar for protection? Starting today, which specific du'a will you make a habit to protect yourself and your loved ones?
?
You now know that hasad is prohibited by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. Has your heart truly submitted to this ruling, or do you still allow envy to linger? What practical step will you take this week to purify your heart from hasad?

How to Cure Envy: Contemplation and Adab with Allah
Prescribes cure for hasad and includes illustrative poems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part cure for hasad: reflecting on the punishment and recognizing it as bad manners towards Allah.

Core Idea
The cure for hasad is to fear Allah's punishment and to realize that envy is bad adab with Allah's decree.

After explaining the prohibition and harms of hasad, the author turns to its treatment. He presents a two-fold cure: first, to contemplate the divine threat against envy, and second, to recognize that envy implies improper conduct towards Allah, as if one is displeased with Allah's distribution of blessings. He illustrates this with poetry and wisdom sayings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements of the cure
Find the two parts: looking at the threat (al-wa‘īd) and realizing it is bad adab with Allah.
2
Read the poem
The poem emphasizes the bad manners aspect and the consequence that the envier is blocked from obtaining blessings.
Note the meter (al-Mutaqarib).
3
Understand the wisdom sayings
The aphorism 'the envious does not become a master' and the poem by Abu Hanifa show the futility of envy.

قوله : (وَدَاءِ الحَسَدِ) أي : داء هو الحسد ; فالإضافة للبيان، هذا إن أريد الداء المعنوي، فإن أُريد الداء الحسي كان من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه; أي: الحسد الشبيه بالداء، وهو تمني زوال نعمة الغير ; سواء تمناها لنفسه أو لا، بأن تمنى انتقالها عن غيره لغيره، وهذا أخس الأخساء; لأنه باع آخرته بدنيا غيره ; بخلاف ما إذا تمنى مثل نعمة الغير، فإنه غِبْطَةٌ محمودة في الخير، كما ورد : لا حسد إلا في اثنتين ... الحديث . و دليل تحريمه الكتاب والسنة والإجماعُ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِن شَرِّ حَاسِدٍ إِذَا حَسَدَ﴾ [الفلق]، وشره كثير، فمنه ما هو غير مكتسب، وهو إصابة العين ومنه : ما هو مكتسب; كسعيه في تعطيل الخير عنه، وتنقيصه عند الناس، وربما دعا عليه أو بطش به، إلى غير ذلك. وقال : إياكم والحسد; فإنَّ الحسد يأكل الحسنات، كما تأكل النار الحطب أو العُشْبَ. ودواء الحسد النظر للوعيد مع أنه إساءة أدب مع الله تعالى; كأنه لا يُسلَّمُ له حكمه، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من المتقارب] أَلا قُلْ لِمَنْ باتَ لي حاسداً : أَتَدْرِي عَلَى مَنْ أَسَأْتَ الأَدَبْ؟ اَسَأتَ عَلَى اللهِ فِي فِعْلِهِ كَأَنَّكَ لَمْ تَرْضَ لِيْ مَا وَهَبْ فَكَانَ جَزَاؤُكَ أَنْ خَصَّنِي وَسَدَّ عَلَيْكَ طَرِيقَ الطَّلَبْ ومن الحكمة : الحسود لا يَسودُ ; أي: كثير الحسد لا تحصل له سيادة. ومن كلام أبي حنيفة رضي الله تعالى عنه : [من البسيط] إِنْ يَحْسُدُونِي فَإِنِّي غَيْرُ لَائِمِهِمْ قَبْلِي مِنَ النَّاسِ أَهْلُ الفَضْلِ قَدْ حُسِدُوا فَدَامَ لِيْ وَلَهُمْ مَا بِيْ ومَا بِهِمُ ومَاتَ أَكْثَرُنَا فَيْظاً بِما يَجِدُ أنَا الَّذِي يَجِدُونِي فِي صُدُورِهِم لَا أَرْتَقِيْ صَدَراً مِنْها وَلَا أَرِدُ ويُروى أنَّ إبليس قال لسيدنا نوح عليه الصلاة والسلام: (خذ خمساً، قال: لا أصدِّقُك، فأوحى الله إليه أن صَدِّقُهُ، فقال : قل; فقال : إياك والكبر، فإني إنما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه بالكبر، وإياك والحسد، فإن قابيل قتل أخاه هابيل بالحسد، وإياك والطمع، فإن آدم ما أورثه الله ما أورثه إلا بالطمع، وإياك والحرص، فإن حواء ما وقعت فيما وقعت فيه إلا بالحرص، وإياك وطول الأمل، فإنهما ما وقعا فيما وقعا فيه إلا بطول الأمل).
His statement: (and the disease of envy) means: a disease that is envy; so the addition is for clarification, this if what is meant is the figurative disease; but if what is meant is the physical disease, it is of the type of addition of the simile to the compared; i.e., envy is similar to a disease. Five Traits from Iblis: Pride, Envy, Greed, Covetousness, Prolonged Hope
Relates Iblis's warning to Nuh about five destructive traits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the five destructive traits that Iblis taught Prophet Nuh, with envy being one of them, and their origins in major sins.

Core Idea
Iblis warned Nuh against five traits: pride, envy, greed, covetousness, and prolonged hope—each traced back to a major sin.

The passage concludes with a powerful story: Iblis himself lists five traits that lead to destruction. He admits that pride caused his own fall, envy caused Qabil to kill Habil, greed caused Adam's trial, covetousness caused Hawwa's sin, and prolonged hope led to their persistence. This serves as a stark warning against these spiritual diseases.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the narration about Iblis and Nuh. Note the five traits and their consequences.
2
Identify each trait and its example
For each trait, find the example given: pride (Iblis), envy (Qabil/Habil), greed (Adam), covetousness (Hawwa), prolonged hope (both).
3
Reflect on envy in this list
Envy is placed second. How does this connect to the earlier lessons?
Envy led to the first murder.
4
Consider the lesson
Why would Iblis reveal these? What does it teach us about the gravity of these traits?
Iblis wants to harm humans by revealing these traps.

Prophetic Guidance Outweighs Others
Commentary on first hemistich of couplet 138.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's practices (hady) are more weighty than any other guidance, and the three categories of his practices that are not meant for universal emulation.

Core Idea
The Prophet's practice always outweighs others, but not every practice is for us.

The poet declares that every practice of the Prophet is more weighty than any other saying or deed. The commentator explains the meaning and delineates which of the Prophet's practices are to be followed and which are not, such as those abrogated or specific to him. Let's read the passage to grasp this foundational principle of following the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the couplet and the commentator's explanation. Notice how he first defines 'hady' then lists exceptions.
2
Identify the three exceptions
The commentator mentions three types of prophetic practices not meant for emulation: abrogated practices, those merely showing permissibility, and practices specific to the Prophet.
For example, the Prophet's ability to pray the whole night was abrogated.
3
Understand the weight concept
The phrase 'qad rajaḥ' means the Prophet's practice is superior to others, not just permissible. This establishes priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣٨ - وَكُلُّ هَدْيِ لِلنَّبِيِّ قَدْ رَجَحْ فَمَا أُبِيْحَ افْعَلْ وَدَعْ مَا لَمْ يُبَحْ
١٣٩ - فَتَابِعِ الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ مِمَّنْ خَلَفَا
١٤٠ - هَذَا وَأَرْجُو اللهَ فِي الإِخْلاصِ مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ ثُمَّ فِي الخَلاصِ
قوله : (وَكُلُّ هَدْيِ لِلنَّبِيِّ قَدْ رَجَحْ) أي : وكلُّ هَدْي منسوب للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد رَجَعَ على ما لم ينسب له من الأقوال والأفعال والاعتقادات، فأفضل الأحوال أحواله التي لم تُنسخ ، وليس المقصود بها مجرد بيان الجواز، ولا مما قام الدليل علی اختصاصه به ، بخلاف ما نُسخ كقيام الليل كله، وما قصد به مجرد بیان الجواز كوضوئه مرَّةٌ مرَّةٌ، وما كان مُختَصّا به عليه الصلاة والسلام؛ كتزوجه أكثر من أربع .
قوله : (فَمَا أُبِيْحَ افْعَلْ) أي : فما لم يُنْهَ عنه، ولو تنزيهاً افعل، فالمراد بما أبيح ما لم ينه عنه، فيشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح ، وهو ما استوى طرفاه؛ أي: فعله وتركُه .
138 - And every practice (hady) of the Prophet is more weighty (qad rajaḥ), so do what is permitted and leave what is not permitted.
139 - So follow the righteous among those who preceded and avoid the innovation of those who came later.
140 - This, and I ask God for sincerity against hypocrisy, then for salvation.
His saying: (وَكُلُّ هَدْيِ لِلنَّبِيِّ قَدْ رَجَحْ) i.e. Every practice attributed to the Prophet (peace be upon him) is more weighty than what is not attributed to him of sayings, deeds, and beliefs. So the best states are his states that were not abrogated. And the intended is not merely the clarification of permissibility, nor of what evidence has established as specific to him, contrary to what was abrogated, like standing the entire night in prayer, and what was intended merely as a clarification of permissibility, like his ablution once each, and what was specific to him (peace and blessings be upon him), like his marrying more than four.
His saying: (فَمَا أُبِيْحَ افْعَلْ) i.e. So what is not forbidden, even if it is only disliked, do. So what is meant by 'what is permitted' is what is not forbidden, and it includes the obligatory, the recommended, and the permissible. And the permissible is that whose two ends are equal, i.e. doing it and leaving it.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents three couplets from the poem followed by commentary on the first. The first couplet (138) states: 'Every practice of the Prophet is more weighty, so do what is permitted and leave what is not.' The commentator explains that 'hady' means any action, saying, or belief attributed to the Prophet; these are more weighty (رَجَعَ) than anything else not attributed to him. The best states are those that were not abrogated. The phrase 'قد رجح' indicates priority, not just permissibility. The commentator distinguishes three categories: (1) practices that were abrogated (like standing the whole night in prayer) are not to be followed, (2) practices that merely show permissibility (like the Prophet's ablution once each) are not necessarily recommended, and (3) practices specific to the Prophet (like marrying more than four) are not for the community. The second hemistich is then explained: 'فما أبيح افعل' means to do whatever is not forbidden, even if it is only disliked (makruh tanzihan). The term 'mubah' here is used broadly to include the obligatory (wajib), recommended (mandub), and permissible (mubah proper) — anything without a prohibition. The permissible proper is defined as that whose doing and leaving are equal. The latter two couplets (139-140) are included in the Arabic text but not commented on in this window; they enjoin following the righteous Salaf, avoiding bid'ah, and seeking sincerity and salvation from God.

Key Terms
هدي
رجح
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think every action of the Prophet must be emulated equally.
Correction: The commentator shows that abrogated, merely permissive, and Prophet-specific practices are not for our emulation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which categories of the Prophet's practices are NOT meant for universal emulation?
A) Only abrogated practices
B) Only practices specific to the Prophet
C) Abrogated, those showing permissibility, and specific to the Prophet
D) All practices are to be followed

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's practices are superior, but only those not abrogated, not merely permissive, and not specific to him are universal models.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you ever assume that everything the Prophet did is automatically a sunnah to follow? What habit or practice do you now realize you need to investigate further to see if it truly applies to you? Will you take the time today to research one such practice?
?
The passage shows that some actions of the Prophet were merely to show permissibility, not to encourage emulation. Is there any practice you do simply because the Prophet did it, without checking if it was a recommended sunnah? Can you be honest with yourself and commit to learning the ruling behind it?
?
If you truly believe that the Prophet's guidance is more weighty than anything else, how does that affect your daily choices? What is one area of your life where you prioritize your own opinion or cultural tradition over the authentic sunnah? What will you change starting tomorrow?

Do What is Permitted: The Broad Meaning of Mubah
Commentary on second hemistich of couplet 138.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in this context 'what is permitted' (mubah) includes all acts not forbidden—obligatory, recommended, and neutral—and the technical definition of mubah proper.

Core Idea
What is permitted here is whatever is not prohibited, covering all halal actions.

The second hemistich commands: 'do what is permitted.' The commentator clarifies that 'permitted' here means anything not forbidden, widening the scope to include obligatory and recommended acts. Let's see how the definition unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on the second phrase
Focus on the commentator's explanation of 'فَمَا أُبِيْحَ افْعَلْ'.
2
Understand the inclusive definition
Note that 'mubah' is defined as whatever is not forbidden, including wajib, mandub, and truly mubah.
This is a non-technical use of mubah.
3
Distinguish the true mubah
The commentator adds that the truly permissible is that whose doing and leaving are equal.
This is the technical definition.
