Leaving the Forbidden and Following the Righteous Predecessors
The commentator explains commands to leave forbidden actions and follow the pious predecessors, citing a hadith and defining righteousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of abandoning forbidden actions and following the righteous predecessors in belief, speech, and action.

Core Idea
Following the Salaf means adhering to the Sunnah with unwavering commitment.

This passage from the commentary explains two key commands: to leave what is not permitted and to follow the righteous from the early generations. The author emphasizes that abandonment includes all categories of prohibition, and following the Salaf is demonstrated by the Prophet's command to cling to his Sunnah and that of the rightly-guided caliphs. Understanding who the 'righteous' are is crucial—they are those who fulfill both divine and human obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two commands
Read the Arabic passage and identify the two commands: 'leave what is not permitted' and 'follow the righteous from those who preceded'. Notice how they are connected.
The commands are presented in the poem lines being commented on.
2
Understand the scope of prohibition
The commentator explains that 'what is not permitted' includes haram, makruh, and khilaf al-awla. Reflect on why all these categories are included.
This broadens the concept of avoidance beyond just the unlawful.
3
Study the hadith and definition of righteous
Read the hadith about adhering to the Sunnah and the caliphs. Note the metaphor 'bite onto it with your molar teeth'. Then learn the definition of 'al-salih' as one who fulfills rights of Allah and people.
The rarity of such righteousness is emphasized with 'red sulfur'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات وقوله : (وَدَعْ مَا لَمْ يُبَحْ) أي : واترك ما لم يبحْ لك فعله ؛ وهو المنهي عنه كان مُحرَّماً أو مكروهاً أو خلاف الأولى. بان قوله : (فَتَابع الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا) أي: فتابع في عقائدك وأقوالك وأفعالك الفريق الصالح ممن سلف، كقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام عليكم بسنتي وسنة الخلفاء الراشدين من بعدي، عَضُّوا عليها بالنواجذه ، وهذا كناية عن شدَّة التمسك بها، والصالح: هو القائم بحقوق الله وحقوق العباد ، وهذا أندر من الكبريت الأحمر، ويُطلق الصالح على النبي كما يطلق على الولي، إلا أن الصلاح في الأنبياء أكمل منه في الأولياء. قوله : (وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ مِمَّنْ خَلَفَا :أي واتركِ البدعة المذمومة ممن جاء بعد خواص الصحابة وعلمائهم . وقد علمت أن البدعة تعتريها الأحكام الخمسة . والحاصل أن كل ما وافق الكتاب والسنة أو الإجماع أو القياس فهو سُنَّةٌ. وما خرج عن ذلك فهو بدعة مذمومةٌ.
Al-Sami'yyat (scriptural matters). And his statement: 'And leave what is not permitted' meaning: and leave what is not permitted for you to do; it is what is prohibited, whether it is forbidden, disliked, or contrary to the better. [بَان] (it became clear?) His statement: 'So follow the righteous from those who preceded' meaning: so follow in your beliefs, statements, and actions the righteous group from those who preceded, like his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Adhere to my Sunnah and the Sunnah of the rightly-guided caliphs after me, bite onto it with your molar teeth' – this is a metaphor for strong adherence. And al-salih (the righteous) is the one who fulfills the rights of Allah and the rights of the servants, and this is rarer than red sulfur. And 'al-salih' is applied to the prophet as it is applied to the saint, except that righteousness in the prophets is more complete than in the saints. His statement: 'And avoid innovation from those who came after' meaning: and leave the blameworthy innovation from those who came after the elite of the Companions and their scholars. And you have known that innovation is subject to the five rulings. And the conclusion is that everything that agrees with the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, or analogy is Sunnah, and whatever deviates from that is blameworthy innovation.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on lines of the poem dealing with obligations and prohibitions in religious practice. It begins by explaining the line 'وَدَعْ مَا لَمْ يُبَحْ' (and leave what is not permitted) as a command to abandon anything forbidden, whether haram, makruh, or khilaf al-awla. The commentator then clarifies the phrase 'فَتَابع الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا' (so follow the righteous from those who preceded) as a command to follow the pious predecessors in beliefs, words, and deeds. He quotes the hadith urging adherence to the Sunnah of the Prophet and the rightly-guided caliphs. The term 'salih' (righteous) is defined as one who fulfills both the rights of Allah and the rights of people, noted to be extremely rare; it can apply to prophets and saints, but the righteousness of prophets is superior. Next, the commentator interprets 'وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ مِمَّنْ خَلَفَا' (and avoid innovation from those who came after) as a command to avoid blameworthy innovations introduced after the generation of the Companions and their scholars. He reminds that innovations can fall under any of the five legal rulings (obligatory, recommended, etc.). The key conclusion (al-hasil) is stated: whatever aligns with the Quran, Sunnah, consensus (ijma'), or analogy (qiyas) is Sunnah; whatever deviates from these is blameworthy innovation (bid'ah madhmumah). This provides a comprehensive criterion for distinguishing orthodoxy from innovation.

Key Terms
سلف
سنة
صالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'leave what is not permitted' only refers to haram acts.
Correction: According to the commentary, it includes makruh and khilaf al-awla as well.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the command 'leave what is not permitted' include?
A) Only haram actions
B) Haram, makruh, and khilaf al-awla
C) Only actions that are clearly sinful
D) Only actions of innovation

Key Takeaway
Abandoning forbidden actions and following the Salaf in all aspects of faith and practice are foundational to Sunni orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command to leave something, do you only avoid the clearly haram, or do you also consider the makruh and the less preferable? What step will you take today to investigate an action you were unsure about and abandon it if it falls under any of these categories?
?
The passage says the righteous are extremely rare, like red sulfur. Do you truly seek to be among them, or do you settle for a minimal standard of piety? What specific right of Allah or right of a fellow human are you neglecting that prevents you from being among the salihin?
?
The hadith commands biting onto the Sunnah with molar teeth. In which area of your life—beliefs, words, or actions—are you holding onto the Sunnah most loosely? What concrete change will you make this week to grip it more firmly?

Avoiding Innovation and Recognizing Sunnah
The commentator explains the command to avoid innovation, its five rulings, and the criterion distinguishing Sunnah from bid'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of blameworthy innovation and the criterion for distinguishing Sunnah from bid'ah.

Core Idea
Sunnah is everything that aligns with Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', or Qiyas; everything else is blameworthy innovation.

The commentator now turns to the command to avoid innovation introduced after the generation of the Companions. He reminds that innovations can fall under any of the five legal rulings, and then provides a clear criterion: whatever agrees with the fundamental sources is Sunnah, and whatever deviates is blameworthy innovation. This establishes a methodology for evaluating religious practices.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command to avoid innovation
Read the passage: 'avoid innovation from those who came after'. Note that it specifies 'blameworthy innovation' and that the people after the Companions are referred to.
Innovation is not automatically condemned; it is subject to rulings.
2
Explore the five rulings on innovation
The commentator says innovation can be subject to the five rulings (obligatory, recommended, etc.). Think about examples like using loudspeakers for adhan (permitted innovation).
This nuance prevents blanket condemnation of all new practices.
3
Learn the criterion for Sunnah vs. bid'ah
The passage concludes with the criterion: whatever agrees with Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', or Qiyas is Sunnah; whatever deviates is bid'ah. Memorize this measure.
This is a comprehensive principle from the later scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'Sunnah' is only what is directly reported in hadith, excluding analogy.
Correction: The passage explicitly includes Qiyas (analogy) as a source of Sunnah.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the criterion for something being considered Sunnah?
A) Only the explicit text of the Quran
B) The Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas
C) Whatever the majority of scholars say
D) Whatever is practiced in a particular region

Key Takeaway
The measure of correct practice is its conformity to the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and sound analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a practice you've always assumed was part of the Sunnah. Have you ever checked whether it truly aligns with the Quran, authentic hadith, scholarly consensus, or sound analogy? If not, what does that say about your commitment to following the Prophet's path?
?
When you encounter a new religious practice, do you instinctively ask, 'Where is this from?' or do you accept it because it feels right? How can you train yourself to always measure it against the four sources mentioned in this lesson?
?
Consider something you do regularly that might be an innovation. Are you willing to abandon it if you find it does not meet the criterion? What would that require of you in terms of humility and obedience?

Seeking Salvation from Shaitan and His Offspring
Author explains meaning of 'al-rajim' and origin of Shaitan's offspring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'al-rajim' and the origin of Shaitan's offspring, and why we must seek Allah's deliverance from them.

Core Idea
Shaitan's enmity against humans originates from his expulsion and his offspring's war against us.

This lesson explores the first of four enemies mentioned in the poem: the accursed shaitan. The author defines what 'rajim' means and explains how Iblis became the father of all devils, establishing a lineage of enmity against humanity. Understanding this helps us heed Allah's command to take shaitan as an enemy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the source, focusing on the verses and their explanation. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the poem lines and the author's commentary.
2
Understand the definition of 'al-rajim'
The author gives two possible meanings: the one stoned/expelled from mercy, or the one who shows false mercy through whispers. Note the linguistic pattern.
This dual meaning enriches our understanding of shaitan's nature.
3
Learn how Iblis produced offspring
The passage narrates that Iblis, after being cast down, committed sodomy with himself and emitted five eggs, becoming the origin of all devils.
This is a unique narrative found in some Islamic sources.
4
Reflect on the Quranic command
Read the verse and the author's explanation: take shaitan as an enemy in beliefs, words, and deeds. Consider how this applies to your life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ فِي الخَلاصِ ... إلخ) أي: وأرجو الله في الخلاص من هذه الأمور، فـ (ثم) هنا وفيما بعد بمعنى الواو، كما يدل عليه تعبير الناظم بالواو في قوله : (والهوى)، وما أحسن قول بعضهم في هذا المعنى: [من الكامل] إِنِّي بُلِيْتُ بِأَرْبَعِ تَرْمِيْتَنِي بالنَّبْلِ قَدْ نَصَبُوا عَلَيَّ شِرَاكَا إِبْلِيسُ وَالدُّنْيَا ونَفْسِي والهَوَى مِنْ أَيْنَ أَرْجُو بَيْنَهُنَّ فِكَاكَا؟! يَا رَبِّ سَاعِدْنِي بِعَفْوِكَ إِنَّنِي أَصْبَحْتُ لَا أَرْجُو لَهُنَّ سِوَاكَا ١٤١ - مِنَ الرَّحِيْمِ ثُمَّ نَفْسِي وَالهَوَى فَمَنْ يَمِلْ لِهَؤُلاءِ قَدْ غَوَى : قوله: (مِنَ الرَّحِيْمِ) أي من الوقوع في مكايدِ الشيطان. والرجيم بمعنى المرجوم؛ أي: المطرود عن رحمة الله تعالى؛ أو بمعنى الرَّاحِم للناس بوسوسته، عنه ، ف رجيم) فعيل بمعنى مفعول أو فاعل، والمراد بالشيطان الرجيم ما يَشْمَل إبليس وأعوانه وهم أولاده من ظهره، فإنه لما أُهْبِط من الجنة لاط بنفسه؛ لكونه لا زوجة له فياض خمس بَيْضاتٍ فكانت أصلَ ذُرِّيَّتِه ، فهو أول مَنْ لاط، كما رُوي وهو أبو الشياطين، كما أن آدم أبو الإنس والعداوة بين الثقلين أعني الإنس والجن فرع العداوة بين الأبوين قال تعالى : (إِنَّ الشَّيْطَنَ لَكُمْ عَدُوٌّ فَاتَّخِذُوهُ عَدُوا [فاطر: ٦]؛ أي : في عقائدكم وأقوالكم وأفعالكم ، وكونوا على حَذَرٍ منه في جميع أحوالكم.
His saying: 'Then in deliverance... etc.' i.e.: I hope from Allah deliverance from these matters. So 'thumma' here and thereafter is in the sense of 'waw', as indicated by the poet's expression with 'waw' in his saying: 'and al-hawa'. How excellent is the saying of someone in this meaning: [al-Kamil] 'I have been afflicted by four, they shot me with arrows, they set up a snare against me: Iblis, the world, my nafs, and al-hawa. From where can I hope for deliverance from them? O Lord, aid me with Your pardon, I have no hope for them except You.' 141 - 'From the Accursed, then my nafs, and al-hawa; whoever inclines to these has gone astray.' His saying: 'From the Accursed' i.e. from falling into the plots of the Shaytan. And 'al-rajim' means the stoned one, i.e., the one expelled from the mercy of Allah the Exalted; or it means the one who shows mercy to people through his whisperings. 'Rajim' is fa'eel in the sense of maf'ool or faa'il. And what is intended by 'al-Shaytan al-rajim' includes Iblis and his helpers, who are his offspring from his back. For when he was cast down from Paradise, he committed sodomy with himself, because he had no wife, so he emitted five eggs, which became the origin of his progeny. So he was the first to commit sodomy, as is narrated, and he is the father of the devils, just as Adam is the father of humans. And the enmity between the two heavy ones, meaning humans and jinn, is a branch of the enmity between the two fathers. The Exalted said: {Indeed, the Shaytan is an enemy to you, so take him as an enemy} [Fatir: 6], i.e., in your beliefs, words, and deeds, and be wary of him in all your states.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a line of the poem in which the poet seeks deliverance from four enemies: Iblis, the world, the self (nafs), and whims (hawa). The author first clarifies that 'thumma' (then) here means 'and', as the poet uses 'waw' in the line. He then quotes a beautiful poem expressing the same idea. The lesson then focuses on the first enemy: 'al-rajim' (the accursed). The author defines 'rajim' as the one stoned and expelled from mercy, or alternatively as the one who shows false mercy through whispers. The word is a verbal noun pattern indicating either passive or active meaning. The shaytan referred to includes Iblis and all his offspring, who were born when Iblis, after being cast from Paradise, committed an indecent act with himself and produced five eggs that became the origin of all devils. Thus he is the father of jinn-devils, just as Adam is father of humans. The enmity between humans and jinn is thus a branch of the enmity between the two fathers. A Quranic verse commands taking Shaytan as an enemy in all aspects – belief, speech, and action – and to be cautious.

Key Terms
الرجيم
الشيطان
إبليس
ذريته
العداوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'rajim' only means stoned/expelled, and that Shaitan is just Iblis personally.
Correction: The author explains that 'rajim' can also mean one who shows false mercy through whispers, and that Shaitan includes Iblis and all his offspring.

Checkpoint
How did Iblis produce offspring according to this passage?
A) By marrying a female jinn.
B) By committing sodomy with himself and producing eggs.
C) By being created with them from fire.
D) By tempting humans to follow him.

Key Takeaway
The root of enmity between humans and jinn is the enmity between Adam and Iblis, and we must be vigilant against shaitan and his offspring in all aspects of life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you truly take shaytan as an enemy in your beliefs, words, and deeds, or have you grown complacent? Which specific area today requires you to actively oppose his plots, and what will you do about it?
?
The enmity between Adam and Iblis extends to their offspring. Are you living as if this war is real, or do you ignore the fact that every choice you make aligns you either with Adam's path or with Iblis's? How will you remind yourself of this battle tomorrow morning?
?
Seeking refuge from shaytan means acknowledging your vulnerability to his plots. Where in your character—pride, anger, laziness—does shaytan find an opening? What concrete step will you take this week to fortify that weakness against his attacks?

The Evil-Commanding and Blaming Souls
Author defines nafs as enemy and lists first two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the nafs as an enemy and the first two types: al-ammara and al-lawwama.

Core Idea
The soul is more deceitful than seventy devils, but struggle can refine it.

The poet begins by seeking deliverance from his nafs, likening it to a shaytan in plotting. The author explains that the nafs referred to here is al-ammara, which commands evil, and then introduces al-lawwama, which reproaches its owner after submission to truth through struggle (mujahada).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the poet's plea and the author's definition of the nafs as an enemy.
2
Identify the two types
Find the descriptions of al-ammara and al-lawwama. Notice how they differ: one always commands evil, the other blames after erring.
The phrase 'bikhilaf' signals a contrast.
3
Reflect on mujahada
Observe that al-lawwama arises 'due to struggle' (bissabab al-mujahada). Consider how spiritual effort transforms the soul.
Mujahada is key to progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ نَفْسِي) أي وأرجو الله في الخلاص من مكايد نفسي التي هي الشيطان في الكيدِ، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من البسيط] أشدُّ من تَوَقَّ نَفْسَكَ لا تَأْمَنْ غَوائِلَها فَالنَّفْسُ أَخْبَتُ مِنْ سَبْعِينَ شَيْطَانَا والمراد بالنفس هنا : الأمارة وهي التي تأمر بالسوء، ولا تأمر بالخير إلا نادراً، بخلاف اللوامَةِ؛ وهي التي تغلب صاحبها ، ثم ترجع عليه باللوم على ما يقع منه؛ لكونها أذعنت للحق بسبب المجاهدة، والمُلْهَمةِ؛ وهي التي ألهمت فجورها وتقواها ؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمُطْمَئِنَّةِ وهي التي اطمأنت إلى مكارم الأخلاق، والراضية؛ وهي التي رضيت بالله ربا من غير منازعة باطنية؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمَرْضِيَّة؛ وهي التي تجلى الله عليها بالرضا والعَفْوِ عمَّا مضى، والكاملة؛ وهي التي صارت الكمالات لها طبعاً وسَجِيَّةٌ، ومع ذلك تترقّى في الكمال، ثم بعد كمال النفس لا يجوز للشخص أن يتصدى للإرشاد إلا بإذن صريح لكن الوقت قد تأخر، فقَلَّ من يتنبه من غفلته، ويصدق في رغبته . فعلى العاقل بالجد والاجتهاد حتى يسير في طريق الرشاد.
His saying: 'Then my nafs' i.e., I hope from Allah for deliverance from the plots of my nafs, which is like the shaytan in plotting. That is why some said: [al-Basit] 'Beware of your nafs, do not feel safe from its calamities, for the nafs is more deceitful than seventy shaytans.' What is intended by nafs here is: al-ammara (the commanding soul), which commands evil and rarely commands good, unlike al-lawwama (the blaming soul), which overcomes its owner but then returns to blaming him for what occurs, because it has submitted to truth through struggle; and al-mulhama (the inspired soul), which is inspired with its wickedness and piety through struggle; and al-mutma'inna (the tranquil soul), which is at peace with noble character; and al-radiyya (the pleased soul), which is content with Allah as Lord without inner opposition through struggle; and al-mardiyya (the pleasing soul), upon which Allah manifests pleasure and forgiveness for the past; and al-kamila (the perfect soul), which has perfections as its nature and disposition, yet still progresses in perfection. Then after the perfection of the nafs, it is not permissible for a person to undertake guidance except with explicit permission, but the time has passed, so few wake up from their heedlessness and are sincere in their desire. So the intelligent person must strive and exert effort until he walks on the path of guidance.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage explains the second enemy: the nafs (self/soul). The author interprets the poet's phrase 'thumma nafsi' as seeking deliverance from the plots of the nafs, which is like a shaytan in its deception. A verse is quoted warning that the nafs is more treacherous than seventy shaytans. The main focus is the classification of the nafs into seven types, attained through spiritual struggle (mujahada). They are: 1) al-ammara (the commanding soul) – always commands evil, rarely good. 2) al-lawwama (the blaming soul) – it overcomes its owner but then reproaches him, indicating some submission to truth. 3) al-mulhama (the inspired soul) – inspired with both good and evil. 4) al-mutma'inna (the tranquil soul) – at peace with noble character. 5) al-radiyya (the pleased soul) – content with Allah without inner conflict. 6) al-mardiyya (the pleasing soul) – Allah is pleased with it and forgives its past. 7) al-kamila (the perfect soul) – perfection becomes its nature, yet it continues to ascend. After reaching perfection, one may not guide others without explicit authorization, but the time for that is rare. The lesson concludes with an exhortation to strive diligently on the path of guidance.

Key Terms
النفس الأمارة
النفس اللوامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nafs is only the evil-commanding soul and cannot change.
Correction: The passage shows the nafs can progress to a blaming stage through struggle, and further purification is possible.

Checkpoint
Which of the two souls rarely commands good?
A) Al-ammara
B) Al-lawwama
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The nafs is a cunning enemy, but through struggle it can transform into a blaming soul that seeks truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you immediately feel a voice inside reproaching you? That voice is your blaming soul (nafs al-lawwama) speaking. What will you do today to listen to it more closely and respond with immediate repentance?
?
Think of a sin you have repeated, justifying it to yourself as 'just my nature.' This lesson says your soul is more deceitful than seventy devils. How will you stop justifying that sin and instead begin the struggle (mujahada) to overcome it?
?
If your soul is currently dominated by the evil-commanding, what is one concrete action you will take within the next 24 hours to weaken its hold and strengthen the blaming soul? Write it down now as a commitment to yourself and Allah.

Higher Stages: From Inspired to Pleasing
Author enumerates four higher types of the soul from mulhama to mardiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the intermediate stages of the soul's purification: al-mulhama, al-mutma'inna, al-radiyya, and al-mardiyya.

Core Idea
Through struggle, the soul ascends from being inspired with good and evil to becoming pleasing to Allah.

After the blaming soul, the author describes four more stages. Each is attained through mujahada and brings the soul closer to perfect tranquility and divine pleasure. This lesson outlines these transformative stations.

Guided Path
1
Read the descriptions
Read the Arabic text and translation for the four souls: al-mulhama, al-mutma'inna, al-radiyya, and al-mardiyya.
Notice the repeated phrase 'bissabab al-mujahada' for some.
2
Trace the progression
Observe how each stage builds on the previous: inspiration, tranquility, contentment, then being pleasing to Allah.
Al-mardiyya is the only stage where Allah is the subject.
3
Understand the role of struggle
Note that al-mulhama and al-radiyya are explicitly linked to mujahada, implying effort is required.
Even the higher stages depend on struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'Then my nafs' i.e., I hope from Allah for deliverance from the plots of my nafs, which is like the shaytan in plotting. That is why some said: [al-Basit] 'Beware of your nafs, do not feel safe from its calamities, for the nafs is more deceitful than seventy shaytans.' How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The source says the highest stage, al-mardiyya, is reached 'because of struggle.' Are you actively striving against your own lower desires today? Or have you become complacent, thinking that divine pleasure comes without effort?
?
Consider the stage al-radiyya: being content with Allah as Lord without internal dispute. Is there any area of your life where you still argue with Allah's decree—perhaps about your past, your circumstances, or your future? What step can you take this week to surrender that area?

The Perfect Soul and Guidance
Author defines the perfect soul and sets condition for guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the perfect soul (al-kamila) and the conditions for guiding others after perfection.

Core Idea
The perfect soul still progresses, and only with explicit permission may one guide others.

The final stage is the perfect soul (al-kamila), which has perfection as its nature but continues to ascend. The author then adds an important note: after reaching perfection, one may not guide others without explicit authorization. The lesson concludes with an exhortation to strive on the path of guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read about al-kamila
Read the description of the perfect soul in Arabic and translation.
Notice that it still progresses despite being perfect.
2
Understand the condition for guidance
The passage states that after perfection, one cannot guide without explicit permission. Consider why this might be.
This highlights the seriousness of spiritual guidance.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with an exhortation to strive and walk the path of guidance, noting the rarity of sincere seekers.
The phrase 'al-aaqil' implies reason as a starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ نَفْسِي) أي وأرجو الله في الخلاص من مكايد نفسي التي هي الشيطان في الكيدِ، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من البسيط] أشدُّ من تَوَقَّ نَفْسَكَ لا تَأْمَنْ غَوائِلَها فَالنَّفْسُ أَخْبَتُ مِنْ سَبْعِينَ شَيْطَانَا والمراد بالنفس هنا : الأمارة وهي التي تأمر بالسوء، ولا تأمر بالخير إلا نادراً، بخلاف اللوامَةِ؛ وهي التي تغلب صاحبها ، ثم ترجع عليه باللوم على ما يقع منه؛ لكونها أذعنت للحق بسبب المجاهدة، والمُلْهَمةِ؛ وهي التي ألهمت فجورها وتقواها ؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمُطْمَئِنَّةِ وهي التي اطمأنت إلى مكارم الأخلاق، والراضية؛ وهي التي رضيت بالله ربا من غير منازعة باطنية؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمَرْضِيَّة؛ وهي التي تجلى الله عليها بالرضا والعَفْوِ عمَّا مضى، والكاملة؛ وهي التي صارت الكمالات لها طبعاً وسَجِيَّةٌ، ومع ذلك تترقّى في الكمال، ثم بعد كمال النفس لا يجوز للشخص أن يتصدى للإرشاد إلا بإذن صريح لكن الوقت قد تأخر، فقَلَّ من يتنبه من غفلته، ويصدق في رغبته . فعلى العاقل بالجد والاجتهاد حتى يسير في طريق الرشاد.
His saying: 'Then my nafs' i.e., I hope from Allah for deliverance from the plots of my nafs, which is like the shaytan in plotting. That is why some said: [al-Basit] 'Beware of your nafs, do not feel safe from its calamities, for the nafs is more deceitful than seventy shaytans.' What is intended by nafs here is: al-ammara (the commanding soul), which commands evil and rarely commands good, unlike al-lawwama (the blaming soul), which overcomes its owner but then returns to blaming him for what occurs, because it has submitted to truth through struggle; and al-mulhama (the inspired soul), which is inspired with its wickedness and piety through struggle; and al-mutma'inna (the tranquil soul), which is at peace with noble character; and al-radiyya (the pleased soul), which is content with Allah as Lord without inner opposition through struggle; and al-mardiyya (the pleasing soul), upon which Allah manifests pleasure and forgiveness for the past; and al-kamila (the perfect soul), which has perfections as its nature and disposition, yet still progresses in perfection. Then after the perfection of the nafs, it is not permissible for a person to undertake guidance except with explicit permission, but the time has passed, so few wake up from their heedlessness and are sincere in their desire. So the intelligent person must strive and exert effort until he walks on the path of guidance.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage explains the second enemy: the nafs (self/soul). The author interprets the poet's phrase 'thumma nafsi' as seeking deliverance from the plots of the nafs, which is like a shaytan in its deception. A verse is quoted warning that the nafs is more treacherous than seventy shaytans. The main focus is the classification of the nafs into seven types, attained through spiritual struggle (mujahada). They are: 1) al-ammara (the commanding soul) – always commands evil, rarely good. 2) al-lawwama (the blaming soul) – it overcomes its owner but then reproaches him, indicating some submission to truth. 3) al-mulhama (the inspired soul) – inspired with both good and evil. 4) al-mutma'inna (the tranquil soul) – at peace with noble character. 5) al-radiyya (the pleased soul) – content with Allah without inner conflict. 6) al-mardiyya (the pleasing soul) – Allah is pleased with it and forgives its past. 7) al-kamila (the perfect soul) – perfection becomes its nature, yet it continues to ascend. After reaching perfection, one may not guide others without explicit authorization, but the time for that is rare. The lesson concludes with an exhortation to strive diligently on the path of guidance.

Key Terms
النفس الكاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once the soul is perfect, one can automatically guide others.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that even after perfection, explicit permission is required to guide.

B) Explicit permission

?
The author laments that few wake up from heedlessness. Are you one of the few? What specific step will you take today to shake off your own heedlessness and walk the path of guidance with sincerity?

The Meaning and Ambiguity of Hawa (Whims)
Author defines hawa and gives positive and negative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual usage of hawa in Islamic theology: its primary blameworthy sense and its occasional praiseworthy sense.

Core Idea
Hawa can lead to Hell or align with truth.

In this passage, the author clarifies the term hawa, which often carries a negative connotation but can sometimes be used positively. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting Quranic verses and hadith. Let's explore the definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Definition
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition of hawa. Note that it is the inclination of the soul even toward destruction.
2
Identify the Two Usages
Find the two distinct usages presented: the absolute negative usage (opposition to truth) and the rare positive usage (inclination toward truth).
The positive example from Aisha is important.
3
Understand the Derivation
Notice the linguistic link: hawa is derived from yahwi (to fall) because blameworthy desire causes falling into Hell.
This etymology reinforces the danger.
4
Reflect on the Misunderstanding
Consider that hawa is not always blameworthy; it can be used for good desires. This nuance is often overlooked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالهَوَى) أي : وأرجو الله في الخلاص من الهوى، وهو بالقصر : ميل النفس إلى مرغوبها ، ولو كان فيه هلاكُها، وإذا أُطلق انصرف إلى الميل إلى خلافِ الحقِّ غالباً، نحو: ﴿وَلَا نَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى [ص: ٢٦]، وقد يُستعمل في الميل للحقِّ، كما في قول السيدة عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها : لا أرى ربَّك إلا يُسارع في هواك (١) ، تخاطبه الله لما نزل قوله تعالى : وتُرجِي مَن نَشَاءُ ..) [الأحزاب: ٥١] الآية . وسُمِّي الأول هوى؛ لأنه يهوي بصاحبه إلى النار.
His saying: 'and al-hawa' i.e., I hope from Allah for deliverance from al-hawa. It (with shortened vowel) is the inclination of the soul towards its desired object, even if it contains its destruction. When used absolutely, it generally refers to inclination toward opposition to truth, as in: {And do not follow [your] desire} [Sad: 26]. It may also be used for inclination toward truth, as in the saying of Lady A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'I do not see your Lord except that He hastens to fulfill your wish' – she addressed that to Allah when His saying {You may defer whom you wish} [al-Ahzab: 51] was revealed. And the first [blameworthy] is called hawa because it plunges (yahwi) its possessor into the Fire.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the third enemy: hawa (whim or desire). The author states that hawa is the soul's inclination toward what it desires, even if that desire leads to destruction. In its absolute sense, it usually refers to the inclination that opposes the truth, as in the Quranic command not to follow hawa. However, hawa can also be used in a praiseworthy sense, as in the hadith where A'isha says that Allah hastens to fulfill the Prophet's wish (hawa) – here meaning a desire aligned with truth. The term 'hawa' is derived from 'yahwi' (to fall), because blameworthy desire causes one to fall into Hellfire.

Key Terms
الهوى
ميل النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think hawa always means sinful desire.
Correction: While hawa usually refers to blameworthy desire opposing truth, it can also refer to rightful inclination, as shown in Aisha's statement about the Prophet.

Checkpoint
What is the linguistic derivation of the word 'hawa' according to the author?
A) From 'hawa' meaning air.
B) It is derived from 'yahwi' (to fall) because it leads to Hell.
C) It means empty space.
D) It means heartfelt longing.

Key Takeaway
Hawa primarily denotes blameworthy desire but can refer to praiseworthy inclination when aligned with truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a desire you actively pursue despite knowing it displeases Allah. What is that desire? When will you finally choose truth over it, knowing it could plunge you into the Fire?
?
The passage shows that even the Prophet had a praiseworthy hawa that Allah hastened to fulfill. Is there any desire in your heart for good—like praying more, giving charity, or seeking knowledge—that you keep postponing? What is stopping you from embracing it as a gift from Allah?
?
You just learned that hawa is the soul's inclination toward what it wants, even to its destruction. Name one specific inclination in your life that you know is destructive but still defend. If you continue defending it, what does that say about your fear of Allah?

The Meaning of 'al-Rajim' and the Reality of Shaytan
Commentator explains 'rajim' and describes shaytan and his offspring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and theological meanings of 'al-rajim' and the scope of 'al-shaytan' as including Iblis and his offspring, as well as the practical implication of taking Satan as an enemy.

Core Idea
Shaytan includes Iblis and his progeny; take him as an enemy in all matters.

This passage unpacks the term 'al-shaytan al-rajim' and reveals that it encompasses not only Iblis but also his offspring. The commentator also relates a unique legend about the origin of these offspring and connects it to the Quranic command to be vigilant against Satan. Let's explore these layers together.

Guided Path
1
Examine the lexical form 'rajim'
Notice that 'rajim' is an intensive form (fa‘īl) that can mean either 'the one who is stoned' or 'the one who stings'. This dual meaning sets the stage for understanding the nature of shaytan.
The form fa‘īl often carries an intensive meaning.
2
Identify the referent of 'al-shaytan al-rajim'
The commentator explicitly states that this phrase includes Iblis and his helpers, who are his offspring from his back. So shaytan is not just a single entity but a lineage.
This expands the common understanding.
3
Reflect on the legendary origin of shaytan's offspring
The text narrates that Iblis, after being cast down, committed sodomy with himself and laid five eggs, which became the origin of his progeny. Reflect on the theological implication that Iblis is the father of the devils.
This story is based on reported narrations.
4
Apply the Quranic command to take shaytan as an enemy
The passage cites the verse 'Indeed, Satan is an enemy to you, so take him as an enemy' and explains that this enmity should be manifest in beliefs, words, and actions, and that believers should be constantly on guard.
This connects the theological teaching to practical life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ف رجيم) فعيل بمعنى مفعول أو فاعل، والمراد بالشيطان الرجيم ما يَشْمَل إبليس وأعوانه وهم أولاده من ظهره، فإنه لما أُهْبِط من الجنة لاط بنفسه؛ لكونه لا زوجة له فياض [؟] خمس بَيْضاتٍ فكانت أصلَ ذُرِّيَّتِه ، فهو أول مَنْ لاط، كما رُوي وهو أبو الشياطين، كما أن آدم أبو الإنس والعداوة بين الثقلين أعني الإنس والجن فرع العداوة بين الأبوين قال تعالى : (إِنَّ الشَّيْطَنَ لَكُمْ عَدُوٌّ فَاتَّخِذُوهُ عَدُوا [فاطر: ٦]؛ أي : في عقائدكم وأقوالكم وأفعالكم ، وكونوا على حَذَرٍ منه في جميع أحوالكم.
(Fa rajim) is an intensive form meaning 'the one who is stoned' or 'the one who stings', and what is meant by 'al-shaytan al-rajim' is that which includes Iblis and his helpers, and they are his offspring from his back. For when he was cast down from Paradise, he committed sodomy with himself; because he had no wife, he laid [؟] five eggs, and they became the origin of his progeny. So he is the first to commit sodomy, as has been narrated, and he is the father of the devils, just as Adam is the father of mankind. The enmity between the two heavy kinds, namely mankind and jinn, is a branch of the enmity between the two fathers. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Satan is an enemy to you, so take him as an enemy' [Fatir: 6]; i.e., in your beliefs, statements, and actions, and be on guard against him in all your states.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological explanation of the term 'al-rajim' in the phrase 'al-shaytan al-rajim'. The commentator first notes that 'rajim' is an intensive form (f'ail) that can be either passive ('the one who is stoned') or active ('the one who stings'). He then clarifies that 'al-shaytan al-rajim' includes not only Iblis but also his helpers, who are his offspring from his back. The commentator relates a legend that when Iblis was cast down from Paradise, he committed sodomy with himself and laid five eggs that became the origin of his progeny. He is therefore the first to commit this act. The enmity between humans and jinn stems from the enmity between Adam and Iblis. The commentator supports this with the Quranic verse from Surat Fatir, which commands believers to take Satan as an enemy in their beliefs, words, and deeds, and to be constantly wary of him. The passage thus defines the term and describes the nature and progeny of shaytan.

Misconception: That 'shaytan' refers only to Iblis and not to any others.
Correction: The commentator explains that 'al-shaytan al-rajim' includes Iblis and his offspring, making shaytan a category that includes Iblis and his helpers.

The Seven Stages of the Soul (Nafs)
The commentator lists seven types of nafs in a classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven stages of the soul (nafs) and how they progress from commanding evil to spiritual perfection.

Core Idea
The soul has seven stages, from commanding evil to perfect submission.

The commentator begins by reminding us of the danger of the nafs, then outlines a spiritual journey through seven distinct stages. Each stage represents a level of purification and closeness to Allah. We will explore each stage and its characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the seven souls
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud. Then read the translation. As you read, note each of the seven names mentioned.
The names are: ammara, lawwama, mulhama, mutma'inna, radiyya, mardiyya, kamila.
2
Understand each definition
For each stage, notice how the commentator defines it. Pay attention to the key characteristics and the role of striving (mujahada) in progressing.
3
Reflect on the progression
Consider how the soul evolves from commanding evil (ammara) to perfect submission (kamila). Ask: What is the spiritual trajectory?
This progression shows the path of self-purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ نَفْسِي) أي وأرجو الله في الخلاص من مكايد نفسي التي هي الشيطان في الكيدِ، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من البسيط] أشدُّ من ... تَوَقَّ نَفْسَكَ لا تَأْمَنْ غَوائِلَها فَالنَّفْسُ أَخْبَتُ مِنْ سَبْعِينَ شَيْطَانَا والمراد بالنفس هنا : الأمارة وهي التي تأمر بالسوء، ولا تأمر بالخير إلا نادراً، بخلاف اللوامَةِ؛ وهي التي تغلب صاحبها ، ثم ترجع عليه باللوم على ما يقع منه؛ لكونها أذعنت للحق بسبب المجاهدة، والمُلْهَمةِ؛ وهي التي ألهمت فجورها وتقواها ؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمُطْمَئِنَّةِ وهي التي اطمأنت إلى مكارم الأخلاق، والراضية؛ وهي التي رضيت بالله ربا من غير منازعة باطنية؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمَرْضِيَّة؛ وهي التي تجلى الله عليها بالرضا والعَفْوِ عمَّا مضى، والكاملة؛ وهي التي صارت الكمالات لها طبعاً وسَجِيَّةٌ، ومع ذلك تترقّى في الكمال، ثم بعد كمال النفس لا يجوز للشخص أن يتصدى للإرشاد إلا بإذن صريح لكن الوقت قد تأخر، فقَلَّ من يتنبه من غفلته، ويصدق في رغبته . فعلى العاقل بالجد والاجتهاد حتى يسير في طريق الرشاد.
His saying: (then my nafs) i.e., I hope for Allah to be saved from the plots of my nafs which is the shaytan in plotting. For that reason, some said: [al-basit] 'More severe than... Beware of your nafs, do not feel safe from its surprises, for the nafs is more wicked than seventy devils.' What is meant by nafs here is the ammara (the commanding soul), which commands evil and only rarely commands good, unlike the lawwama (the blaming soul) which overcomes its owner then returns to blame him for what happens from him because it has submitted to the truth through striving; and the mulhama (the inspired soul) which has been inspired with its wickedness and its piety through striving; and the mutma'inna (the tranquil soul) which has become tranquil with noble character traits; and the radiyya (the contented soul) which is content with Allah as Lord without inner resistance through striving; and the mardiyya (the soul that is pleased with) which Allah has manifested upon it with pleasure and forgiveness for what has passed; and the kamila (the perfect soul) which has perfections as its nature and disposition, and yet it progresses in perfection. Then after the perfection of the soul, it is not permissible for the person to take up guidance except with explicit permission, but time has become late, so few are those who wake from their heedlessness and are truthful in their desire. So the intelligent person must strive and exert effort until he walks the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the seven stages of the soul (nafs) along with a cautionary note about teaching. The commentator begins by explaining the poet's plea for deliverance from the plots of his own nafs, which it calls a kind of shaytan in its plotting. He then quotes a poetic couplet to emphasize the danger of the nafs, which is more wicked than seventy devils. The commentator lists seven types of nafs: (1) al-ammara – the soul that commands evil; (2) al-lawwama – the soul that blames its owner after sin; (3) al-mulhama – the soul inspired with its wickedness and piety through striving; (4) al-mutma'inna – the soul tranquil with noble character; (5) al-radiyya – the soul content with Allah as Lord; (6) al-mardiyya – the soul upon which Allah bestows pleasure and forgiveness; (7) al-kamila – the soul that has perfections as its nature and continues to progress. He then states that after achieving perfection, one may only take up the role of spiritual guide with explicit permission. Unfortunately, few people awaken from their heedlessness and seek guidance sincerely. The wise person must strive diligently to walk the path of guidance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The soul is a single unchanging entity that is either good or bad.
Correction: The soul has seven distinct stages that can transform through striving (mujahada), from commanding evil to perfect submission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You have learned that each stage beyond the first requires striving (mujahada). What is the one area of your soul where you are avoiding that struggle right now—perhaps a habit, a thought pattern, or a relationship? How long will you let it remain at the ammara stage before you engage in the fight?
?
The perfect soul still 'progresses in perfection.' If even the highest soul never stops advancing, where in your life have you become complacent, thinking you have 'arrived'? What step of growth have you been postponing because you considered it unnecessary? Will you take that step before the day ends?

The Caution Against Teaching Without Permission
The commentator warns against teaching without explicit permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a person must not teach or guide others without explicit permission, even after achieving spiritual perfection.

Core Idea
Spiritual perfection does not grant license to teach without permission.

After describing the highest stage of the soul, the commentator issues a serious warning: even a perfected soul must not take up the role of teacher without explicit authorization. This lesson explains the rationale behind this caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning carefully
Read the passage and note the condition for teaching. What is required before one can guide others?
The text says 'لا يجوز للشخص أن يتصدى للإرشاد إلا بإذن صريح'.
2
Understand the rationale
Think about why even a perfected soul needs explicit permission. Consider the potential harm of teaching without authority.
The commentator implies that few are sincere, so permission prevents charlatanism.
3
Apply to personal conduct
Reflect on how this principle applies to modern contexts. Should anyone teach religion without authorization?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ نَفْسِي) أي وأرجو الله في الخلاص من مكايد نفسي التي هي الشيطان في الكيدِ، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من البسيط] أشدُّ من ... تَوَقَّ نَفْسَكَ لا تَأْمَنْ غَوائِلَها فَالنَّفْسُ أَخْبَتُ مِنْ سَبْعِينَ شَيْطَانَا والمراد بالنفس هنا : الأمارة وهي التي تأمر بالسوء، ولا تأمر بالخير إلا نادراً، بخلاف اللوامَةِ؛ وهي التي تغلب صاحبها ، ثم ترجع عليه باللوم على ما يقع منه؛ لكونها أذعنت للحق بسبب المجاهدة، والمُلْهَمةِ؛ وهي التي ألهمت فجورها وتقواها ؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمُطْمَئِنَّةِ وهي التي اطمأنت إلى مكارم الأخلاق، والراضية؛ وهي التي رضيت بالله ربا من غير منازعة باطنية؛ بسبب المجاهدة، والمَرْضِيَّة؛ وهي التي تجلى الله عليها بالرضا والعَفْوِ عمَّا مضى، والكاملة؛ وهي التي صارت الكمالات لها طبعاً وسَجِيَّةٌ، ومع ذلك تترقّى في الكمال، ثم بعد كمال النفس لا يجوز للشخص أن يتصدى للإرشاد إلا بإذن صريح لكن الوقت قد تأخر، فقَلَّ من يتنبه من غفلته، ويصدق في رغبته . فعلى العاقل بالجد والاجتهاد حتى يسير في طريق الرشاد.
His saying: (then my nafs) i.e., I hope for Allah to be saved from the plots of my nafs which is the shaytan in plotting. For that reason, some said: [al-basit] 'More severe than... Beware of your nafs, do not feel safe from its surprises, for the nafs is more wicked than seventy devils.' What is meant by nafs here is the ammara (the commanding soul), which commands evil and only rarely commands good, unlike the lawwama (the blaming soul) which overcomes its owner then returns to blame him for what happens from him because it has submitted to the truth through striving; and the mulhama (the inspired soul) which has been inspired with its wickedness and its piety through striving; and the mutma'inna (the tranquil soul) which has become tranquil with noble character traits; and the radiyya (the contented soul) which is content with Allah as Lord without inner resistance through striving; and the mardiyya (the soul that is pleased with) which Allah has manifested upon it with pleasure and forgiveness for what has passed; and the kamila (the perfect soul) which has perfections as its nature and disposition, and yet it progresses in perfection. Then after the perfection of the soul, it is not permissible for the person to take up guidance except with explicit permission, but time has become late, so few are those who wake from their heedlessness and are truthful in their desire. So the intelligent person must strive and exert effort until he walks the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the seven stages of the soul (nafs) along with a cautionary note about teaching. The commentator begins by explaining the poet's plea for deliverance from the plots of his own nafs, which it calls a kind of shaytan in its plotting. He then quotes a poetic couplet to emphasize the danger of the nafs, which is more wicked than seventy devils. The commentator lists seven types of nafs: (1) al-ammara – the soul that commands evil; (2) al-lawwama – the soul that blames its owner after sin; (3) al-mulhama – the soul inspired with its wickedness and piety through striving; (4) al-mutma'inna – the soul tranquil with noble character; (5) al-radiyya – the soul content with Allah as Lord; (6) al-mardiyya – the soul upon which Allah bestows pleasure and forgiveness; (7) al-kamila – the soul that has perfections as its nature and continues to progress. He then states that after achieving perfection, one may only take up the role of spiritual guide with explicit permission. Unfortunately, few people awaken from their heedlessness and seek guidance sincerely. The wise person must strive diligently to walk the path of guidance.

Key Terms
إذن صريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone who reaches spiritual perfection can automatically teach others without permission.
Correction: Even the perfected soul must obtain explicit permission before teaching, to ensure proper authorization and prevent misguidance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required before a person can take up guidance (irshad)?
A) Spiritual perfection
B) Explicit permission
C) Popular demand
D) Old age

Key Takeaway
Even after spiritual perfection, one must obtain explicit permission before teaching or guiding others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your knowledge or spiritual progress alone entitled you to teach or advise others without explicit permission? How does this passage confront that assumption, and what will you change about your approach to sharing what you know?
?
When you see people in need of guidance, do you feel a sense of urgency that tempts you to act without authorization? In light of this lesson, how can you maintain that urgency while submitting to the requirement for explicit permission?
?
If you are currently teaching or guiding others in any capacity, can you trace a clear permission back to a qualified authority? If not, what concrete step will you take this week to either seek that permission or step back from instructing?

Unseen Matters
beginner
4 min
The Consequence of Inclining to Shaytan, Nafs, and Hawa
The commentator explains that anyone who inclines to these three has gone astray.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that inclining to shaytan, the nafs, and hawa leads to straying from right guidance, and why these three are identified as the source of every trial.

Core Idea
Inclining to shaytan, nafs, or hawa causes departure from right guidance.

This short passage from the commentary explains a critical warning: whoever inclines toward shaytan, the ego, or desire has gone astray. These three are identified as the source of all trials. The commentator explains that this is the reason for the poet's plea for deliverance from them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get a clear sense of the meaning.
Notice the phrase 'هَوْلاء' (these) – it points to something previously mentioned.
2
Identify 'these three'
Recall from the previous lesson that 'these three' refer to al-shaytan al-rajim, the nafs (especially the ammara), and al-hawa. This passage builds on that context.
The commentator explicitly says 'أحد هذه الثلاثة' (any of these three).
3
Understand 'ghawa' and its opposite
The word 'غوى' (strayed) contrasts with 'الرشد' (right guidance) and 'الاستقامة' (uprightness). Understand that inclining to these adversaries leads to loss of spiritual direction.
4
Notice the causal link
The passage states that these three are 'منشأ كل فتنة' (the source of every trial). This explains why inclining to them results in straying: they are the roots of all misguidance.
This is presented as a 'تعليل' (cause) for the poet's earlier statement.
5
Reflect on the warning
Consider why this warning precedes the plea for salvation. Recognizing the danger is the first step to seeking deliverance.
The passage ends by connecting to the poet's next line: 'ثم التي هي الخلاص' (then the salvation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَمَن يَمِل لِهَوْلاء قَد غَوَى أي : لأنَّ كلَّ مُكلَّف يميل لأحد هذه الثلاثة، منشأ كُلِّ فتنة، فقد فارق الرشد وخرج عن الاستقامة، فهذا تعليل لقوله : (ثم التي هي الخلاص إلى آخره).
His saying: (so whoever inclines to these has surely strayed) i.e., because every mukallaf who inclines to any of these three, which are the source of every trial, has departed from right guidance and left uprightness. So this is a cause for his saying: (then the salvation) etc.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This short passage explains the line 'fa-man yamil li-haula' i qad ghawa' – 'so whoever inclines to these has certainly gone astray'. The commentator connects this to the three previously mentioned adversaries: al-shaytan al-rajim, the nafs (especially the ammara), and al-hawa. He states that any accountable person (mukallaf) who inclines to any of these three – which are the source of every trial – has departed from right guidance (rushd) and left uprightness (istiqamah). This is presented as a causal explanation (ta'lil) of the poet's earlier plea for deliverance from these three. The passage thus reinforces the danger of these three sources of misguidance.

Key Terms
هَوْلاء
الرشد
الاستقامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'these three' refers to three unspecified things or a different set of adversaries.
Correction: The passage says 'لِهَوْلاء' (to these), which points to the three adversaries explicitly mentioned in the previous lines: al-shaytan al-rajim, the nafs (especially the ammara), and al-hawa. The commentator confirms this by saying 'أحد هذه الثلاثة' (any of these three).

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does 'فَمَن يَمِل لِهَوْلاء' refer to?
A) Inclining to the three mentioned (shaytan, nafs, hawa)
B) Inclining to worldly desires only
C) Inclining to the devil only
D) Inclining to ignorance

Key Takeaway
Inclining to any of the three adversaries (shaytan, nafs, hawa) is a cause of straying from right guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you are 'inclining' towards your desires (hawa) or following your nafs, even while maintaining a religious appearance? What would it mean for you to have truly departed from rushd in that secret place? Will you turn back today?
?
You say you seek istiqamah, but are you currently leaning towards shaytan's whispers in your doubts or actions? Identify one specific inclination you have been excusing. How does knowing that this very inclination is the source of fitna and straying change your resolve to cut it off?
